Conditions Permitting Breaking Fast: Illness and Fear
Defines 'awarid and begins ruling on illness permitting iftar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of 'arid in Hanafi fiqh and apply the conditions under which a sick person may break the fast during Ramadan.

Core Idea
A sick person who reasonably fears harm from fasting may break the fast; overwhelming presumption suffices.

This lesson introduces the concept of 'awarid (temporary impediments) that permit breaking the fast. The passage defines illness and outlines when fear of increased illness or slow recovery makes it permissible to break the fast. Understanding these conditions helps distinguish between valid excuses and invalid ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'arid
The passage lists various impediments (illness, travel, coercion, etc.). Focus on illness as the primary example.
Illness is defined as a change in constitution towards corruption, first internally, then externally.
2
Understand the definition of illness
Illness is a meaning that alters the body's nature; it includes any pain, wound, or headache. The key is that it threatens harm if fasting continues.
The fear must be of an increase in severity or duration, not just discomfort.
3
Recognize the condition for permissibility
The sick person must have a reasonable fear (based on experience, doctor's advice, or overwhelming presumption) that fasting will worsen the illness or delay recovery.
The text uses 'khar' (fear) which includes both certainty and strong presumption.
4
Note the ruling
Breaking the fast is permissible (ja'iz), but if harm is certain it becomes wajib. The default is permissibility when fear is present.
No kaffarah is required if the fear is genuine; qada' must be made later.
5
See analogous cases
The text extends the same ruling to a fighter facing the enemy, a person with periodic fever or menstruation, and villagers misled by a festival drum. All rely on overwhelming presumption.
In each case, if the presumption was wrong, no kaffarah is owed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض
جمع عارض المرض والسفر والإكراه، والحبل والرضاع والجوع والعطش والهرم
بها يباح الفطر فيجوز لمن خاف وهو مريض زيادة المرض بكم أو كيف لو صام والمرض معنى يوجب تغير الطبيعة إلى الفساد ويحدث أولاً في الباطن، ثم يظهر أثره وسواء كان لوجع عين أو جراحة أو صداع أو غيره أو خاف بطء البرء بالصوم جاز له الفطر لأنه قد يفضي إلى الهلاك فيجب الاحتراز عنه والغازي إذا كان يعلم يقيناً، أو بغلبة الظن القتال بكونه بإزاء العدو، ويخاف الضعف عن القتال وليس مسافر له الفطر قبل الحرب، ومن له نوية حمى أو عادة حيض لا بأس بفطره على ظن وجوده، فإن لم يوجد اختلف في لزوم الكفارة، والأصح عدم لزومها عليهما وكذا أهل الرستاق لو سمعوا الطبل يوم الثلاثين فظنوه عيداً فأفطروا ثم تبين أنه لغيره لا كفارة عليهم و يجوز الفطر الحامل ومرضع خافت على نفسها نقصان العقل أو الهلاك، أو المرض سواء كان على نفسها أو
Section on Impediments
Collective term for the impediment: illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age.
By these, breaking the fast is permitted. So it is permissible for one who fears—being sick—an increase in the illness in quantity or quality if he fasts. And illness is a meaning that changes the nature towards corruption and first occurs internally, then its effect appears; whether it be due to eye pain, a wound, a headache, or other. Or he fears slow recovery from fasting, then it is permissible for him to break the fast because it may lead to destruction, so precaution is necessary. And the fighter if he knows with certainty, or by overwhelming presumption, of fighting because he is facing the enemy, and he fears weakness from fighting, and he is not a traveler, then he may break the fast before battle. And one who has a fever every other day or a custom of menstruation, there is no harm in his breaking the fast based on the presumption of its occurrence. If it does not occur, there is disagreement on the obligation of kaffarah; the most correct is that they are not obligated. Similarly, the villagers if they heard the drum on the thirtieth day and thought it was the festival so they broke the fast, then it became clear that it was for something else, no kaffarah is upon them. And it is permissible for the pregnant woman and the breastfeeding woman who fear for themselves a loss of intellect, or destruction, or illness, whether it be on themselves or...
Source pages p. 683

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the section on impediments (al-'awarid) that permit breaking the fast. The author defines 'awarid as events like illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age. The main ruling: a sick person who fears that fasting will increase his illness (in severity or duration) may break the fast, because illness changes the bodily constitution and may lead to harm. The permission extends to a warrior facing the enemy who fears weakness, even if not traveling; to one who experiences periodic fever or menstrual pain based on strong presumption; and to pregnant or breastfeeding women who fear harm to themselves (mental or physical loss). The text also discusses the case of villagers who break the fast mistakenly on hearing a holiday drum – no kaffarah is required because they had valid presumption. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the ruling is clear: these are valid excuses to not fast.

Key Terms
العارض (al-'āriḍ): An event or condition that arises and permits breaking the fast, such as illness, travel, etc.
المرض (al-maraḍ): A state that alters the natural constitution towards corruption, first internally then externally, whether from eye pain, wound, headache, etc.
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): An expiatory penalty, typically feeding 60 poor or fasting 60 days, required for intentional invalid act of fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an 'arid that permits breaking the fast?
A) Illness
B) Travel
C) Intentional eating without excuse
D) Old age

Key Takeaway
A sick person may break the fast if they reasonably fear harm from fasting; overwhelming presumption of harm is sufficient.

Ruling on Breaking Fast for the Sick Who Fear Harm
Ruling statement with critical refinement on conditions for breaking fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking fast for a sick person, identify the conditions that permit it, and distinguish between permissibility and obligation based on certainty of harm.

Core Idea
A sick person may break fast if they fear increased illness or slow recovery; obligation arises when destruction is certain.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on breaking fast during Ramadan for a sick person who fears harm from fasting. We will explore the conditions that trigger permissibility, the definition of illness in Hanafi law, and a subtle intra-madhhab dispute regarding the healthy person. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly applying the ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled: breaking the fast. The basic ruling is permissibility (mubah) for a sick person who fears harm, but it can become obligatory (wajib) if destruction is certain.
2
Specify the conditions for permissibility
The person must be sick (marad) and fear (khawf) either an increase in the illness (in quantity or quality) or a slow recovery. The fear must be based on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann), not mere speculation.
The text distinguishes between quantity (new illness) and quality (worsening of existing illness).
3
Define the key term 'illness'
Illness is defined as a change in nature towards corruption, initially internal then manifesting externally. It includes various conditions like eye pain, wounds, headaches, etc.
The hashiya quotes al-Qamus to emphasize that illness is darkness and disturbance of nature.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on the healthy person
The healthy person who fears illness from fasting is not permitted to break fast according to the main text and some authorities, but al-Zayla'i disagrees, allowing it. The text does not fully resolve this.
This shows the spectrum of scholarly opinion within the Hanafi school.
5
Reconcile permissibility and obligation
The matn uses 'for one who fears' implying permissibility, but later says 'must guard against it' suggesting obligation. The commentator reconciles: permissibility when destruction is uncertain, obligation when it is certain.
This nuance reflects the Hanafi approach of graded rulings based on level of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بها يباح الفطر فيجوز لمن خاف وهو مريض زيادة المرض بكم أو كيف لو صام والمرض معنى يوجب تغير الطبيعة إلى الفساد ويحدث أولاً في الباطن ثم يظهر أثره وسواء كان لوجع عين أو جراحة أو صداع أو غيره أو خاف بطء البرء بالصوم جاز له الفطر لأنه قد يفضي إلى الهلاك فيجب الاحتراز عنه قوله: (وهو مريض أفاد أن الصحيح الذي غلب على ظنه المرض بصومه ليس له أن يفطر، وأفاد السيد أن في ذلك خلافاً، فالزيلعي على إباحة الفطر له ولعلامة مسكين على عدمه، وقد تبع فيه صاحب الذخيرة، وجرى على إباحة الفطر في الدر وذكر في القهستاني أنّ الممرض ملحق بالمريض قوله : (بكم) المراد بالكم أن ينشأ بالصوم مرض آخر وليس المراد به زيادة الأيام، وإلا تكرر مع قوله أو خاف بطء البرء . قوله : (أو) كيف) بأن يحدث بالصوم اشتداد في المرض القائم قوله : (والمرض معنى الخ) قال في القاموس : المرض ظلام الطبيعة، واضطرابها بعد صفائها واعتدالها اهـ ويقال في اسم الفاعل مارض ومرض، ومريض ا هـ. قوله : ( ويحدث أولاً في الباطن الخ) قال في القاموس : المرض بالفتح للقلب خاصة، وبالتحريك، أو كلاهما الشك والنفاق والفتور والظلمة والنقصان قوله : (أو غيره) كفساد العضو - قوله : ( فيجب الاحتراز عنه هذا يقتضي وجوب الإفطار، وهو ينافي التعبير باللام في قوله : لمن خاف، ويمكن الجمع بأن الجواز عند عدم تحقق الهلاك، والوجوب عند تحققه، وسيأتي في المسافر نظيره
It permits breaking the fast for one who fears, being sick, an increase of illness, whether in quantity or quality if he fasts. Illness is a meaning that necessitates a change of nature to corruption, occurring first internally then its effect appears, whether from eye pain, wound, headache, or other. Or he fears slow recovery from fasting, then it is permitted for him to break the fast because it may lead to destruction, so he must guard against it. Commentary: (He said: 'He is a sick person' benefits that the healthy person who thinks it likely he will become ill by fasting does not have the right to break fast, and al-Sayyid stated there is a difference of opinion: al-Zayla'i permits it for him, but 'Allamah Miskin holds it is not; al-Dhakhirah followed him, and it is held in al-Durr that it is permissible, and it is mentioned in al-Qahastani that one who causes illness is treated like the sick. (He said: 'in quantity') means that fasting causes another illness, not increase in days, otherwise it would be repeated with his saying 'or fears slow recovery'. (He said: 'or quality') means fasting causes severity in the existing illness. (He said: 'illness is a meaning etc.') al-Qamus said: illness is darkness of nature and its disturbance after its purity and moderation. (He said: 'occurs first internally etc.) al-Qamus said: illness with fatha is specifically for the heart, with tashkeel or both are doubt, hypocrisy, languor, darkness, deficiency. (He said: 'or other') like corruption of the limb. (He said: 'so he must guard against it') this necessitates the obligation of breaking the fast, which contradicts the expression 'for one who fears' in his saying, and it can be reconciled: permissibility when destruction is not certain, obligation when it is certain, and a similar point will come in the section on the traveler.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Maraqi al-Falah on the permissibility of breaking the fast for a sick person who fears harm from fasting. The hashiya by al-Tahtawi critically refines and expands upon the matn. The ruling clearly states that a sick person who fears an increase in illness—whether a new illness or a worsening of an existing one—or a delay in recovery may break the fast. The underlying rationale is the potential for destruction (halak), which must be avoided. The commentary clarifies that the healthy person who merely imagines becoming ill is not entitled to break the fast, but there is a dispute among Hanafi authorities: Al-Zayla'i permits it, while others restrict it. The definition of illness is discussed, quoting al-Qamus, emphasizing that illness is a corruption of nature. A subtle point arises: the matn says 'for one who fears' implying permissibility, but the phrase 'must guard against it' seems to suggest obligation. The commentator reconciles this: permissibility applies when destruction is not certain, but obligation when destruction is certain. This is a typical Hanafi nuanced approach to levels of obligation based on certainty of harm.

Key Terms
مرض (marad): A condition that changes nature toward corruption, beginning internally then manifesting externally; includes eye pain, wounds, headache, etc.
خاف (khāfa): To fear, based on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann) from experience or expert opinion, not mere imagination.
بطء البرء (buṭ' al-bari'): Slow recovery from illness; an additional valid reason to break fast alongside fear of increased illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling for a sick person who fears that fasting may cause a slow recovery but does not fear immediate death?
A) It is obligatory to break the fast
B) It is permissible to break the fast
C) It is prohibited to break the fast
D) It is obligatory to continue fasting

Key Takeaway
Breaking the fast for a sick person is permissible when there is a genuine fear of increased illness or slow recovery, and it becomes obligatory when destruction is certain.

Breaking the Fast for the Ghazi and Those with Habitual Conditions
States permissibility for warriors and those with habitual conditions to break fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a warrior, a person with habitual fever or menses, or a villager may break the Ramadan fast based on predominant probability, and explain the Hanafi ruling on kaffarah when the presumed condition does not occur.

Core Idea
Breaking fast based on predominant probability is valid, and no kaffarah is required if the presumption proves false.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies specific allowances for breaking the Ramadan fast in cases of jihad and recurring conditions. It establishes that permissibility hinges on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann), not certainty, and addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding kaffarah when the anticipated event does not materialize. Understanding this ruling helps distinguish between legitimate excuse and willful violation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of people discussed
The passage covers: (1) the ghazi (warrior) facing the enemy, (2) the person expecting fever or menses based on habit, and (3) villagers who hear the Eid drum on the thirtieth day and break fast mistakenly. For each, note the ruling and conditions.
All three are excused based on predominant probability, not certainty.
2
Determine the condition for the warrior
The warrior must know with certainty (yaqin) or by predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann) that he will fight, fear weakness from fasting, and not be a traveler. If he is a traveler, he may break without this excuse.
The condition of 'not being a traveler' ensures this ruling is specific to the non-traveler warrior; travelers already have a general concession.
3
Analyze the ruling for persons with habitual conditions
A person who habitually experiences fever or menses may break the fast based on the predominant probability (nawiyyat) of its occurrence. The text says 'there is no harm' (la ba'sa), indicating permissibility, but it is preferable not to break until certainty.
The ruling applies to both males (fever) and females (menses). The masculine pronoun is generic.
4
Understand the kaffarah dispute and the relied-upon position
If the presumed condition (fever, menses, or Eid) does not occur, there is disagreement within the Hanafi school about whether kaffarah is required. The text declares the most correct view (al-asah) that no kaffarah is due, and this is the mufta bihi position as per al-Durr.
The rationale: the person did not intend transgression (jinaayah); they acted on a valid presumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغازي إذا كان يعلم يقيناً، أو بغلبة الظن القتال بكونه بإزاء العدو، ويخاف الضعف عن القتال وليس مسافر له الفطر قبل الحرب، ومن له نوية حمى أو عادة حيض لا بأس بفطره على ظن وجوده، فإن لم يوجد اختلف في لزوم الكفارة، والأصح عدم لزومها عليهما وكذا أهل الرستاق لو سمعوا الطبل يوم الثلاثين فظنوه عيداً فأفطروا ثم تبين أنه لغيره لا كفارة عليهم قوله: (بكونه) أي بسبب وجوده بمقابلة العدو. قوله: (ويخاف القتال) أي بالصوم. قوله : (وليس) مسافراً) أما المسافر فيجوز له الفطر يغير عذر . قوله: (ومن له الخ) يعم الذكر والأنثى والتذكير في له نظراً للفظ من قوله : (لا بأس بفطره) أفاد أن الأولى أن لا يفطرا حتى يتحققا وعلل في الشرح جواز الفطر بأن ما ذكر بحكم الغلبة كالكائن. قوله : ( والأصح عدم لزومها عليهما) وكذا هو المعتمد في الغازي كما في الدر. قوله : (وكذا أهل الرستاق) أي القرى إذا سمعوا صوت طبل أمير مدينة ذلك الرستاق على ما جرت به عادتهم أنهم يضربونه يوم العيد قوله: (أنه لغيره) أي أن ضرب الطبل لغير العيد كأن كان لفرح. قوله: (لا كفارة (عليهم لأنهم لم يقصدوا الجناية.
And the warrior, if he knows with certainty or by predominant probability of combat because he is facing the enemy, and he fears weakness in combat, and he is not a traveler, he may break the fast before the battle. And the one who has a fever or habit of menstruation, there is no harm in his breaking the fast based on the presumption of its occurrence, and if it does not occur, there is disagreement concerning the obligation of kaffarah, and the most correct view is that it is not obligatory for them. Likewise, the people of the village if they hear the drum on the thirtieth day and think it is the Eid, so they break the fast, then it becomes clear that it was for something else, no kaffarah is upon them. Commentary: (He said: 'due to his being') i.e., due to his presence facing the enemy. (He said: 'and he fears combat') i.e., due to fasting. (He said: 'and he is not a traveler') as for the traveler, it is permissible for him to break the fast without an excuse. (He said: 'the one who has etc.') it includes male and female, and the masculine in 'lahu' is due to the expression 'man'. (He said: 'there is no harm in his breaking the fast') it benefits that it is preferable that they do not break the fast until they are certain, and it is explained in the sharh that the permissibility of breaking the fast is because what is mentioned is treated as predominant like the actual occurrence. (He said: 'the most correct view is that it is not obligatory upon them') and this is the relied-upon view for the warrior as well, as in al-Durr. (He said: 'likewise the people of the village') i.e., the villages if they hear the sound of the drum of the amir of that village, as is their custom that they beat it on the day of Eid. (He said: 'that it was for something else') i.e., the beating of the drum was for other than Eid, like for celebration. (He said: 'no kaffarah is upon them') because they did not intend transgression.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage continues the ruling statement on permissible breakers of fast, focusing on the warrior (ghazi) and those with recurring conditions (fever or menstrual habit). The matn states that a warrior who is certain or predominantly thinks he will fight and fears weakness from fasting may break the fast before battle, provided he is not a traveler. The hashiya clarifies that a traveler may break the fast even without an excuse. For a person expecting fever or menses, it is permissible to break the fast on the presumption of its occurrence; but if the expected condition does not materialize, there is a dispute regarding kaffarah. The text declares the most correct view (asah) that no kaffarah is required for them or for the villagers who break the fast based on a misleading drum sound. The commentary reinforces that the permissibility is based on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann), not certainty, and that the default action is not to break until certainty unless there is strong indication. The source of the authority is al-Durr, indicating it is a relied-upon ruling in the later tradition.

Key Terms
غازي (ghāzī): A warrior engaged in jihad, facing the enemy, who fears weakness from fasting in battle.
نوية حمى (nawiyyat ḥummā): Expectation or recurring habit of fever.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for breaking a Ramadan fast without valid reason; here discussed for those who break based on presumption but are wrong.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when villagers hear the drum on the thirtieth day and break their fast, thinking it is Eid, but later find out the drum was for another occasion, what is the ruling?
A) They must compensate with qada and kaffarah
B) They must only do qada
C) No kaffarah is upon them
D) They must pay kaffarah only if they were certain

Key Takeaway
Breaking the fast based on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann) of a valid excuse is permissible, and no kaffarah is required if the excuse does not materialize, as the person did not intend transgression.

Ruling for Pregnant and Breastfeeding Women Fearing Harm
States permissibility for pregnant and breastfeeding women to break fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for pregnant and breastfeeding women regarding fasting, apply the conditions of fear of harm to determine when the concession applies, and refute the restricted view limiting it to hired wet nurses.

Core Idea
Pregnant and breastfeeding women may break the fast if they fear harm to themselves or their child.

This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling that pregnant and breastfeeding women are permitted to break the fast when they genuinely fear harm. The passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the scope of this concession, refuting a restrictive view that limits it to hired wet nurses, and provides the key hadith proof. Understanding this ruling helps in applying the correct conditions and recognizing the legal basis for the concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states it is permissible (yajuz) for a pregnant or breastfeeding woman to break the fast under specific conditions. Recognize that the default hukm for a capable person is obligation, but here a concession (rukhsah) is given.
2
Extract the conditions for the concession
The ruling is conditional on fear (khawf) of harm: deficiency of intellect, destruction (halak), or illness. This fear can be for herself or for her child (whether biological or through breastfeeding).
3
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text cites the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has lifted from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman the fast.' This is the primary evidence.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and the Hanafi response
Some scholars restrict the concession to a hired wet nurse. The Hanafi position, relying on the general wording of the hadith and the obligatory nature of breastfeeding, rejects this restriction as 'mardood' (refuted).
5
Understand the practical implication (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: under the restrictive view, only hired wet nurses can break fast; under the Hanafi view, any breastfeeding woman (including the mother) may do so, provided she fears harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز الفطر للحامل ومرضع خافت على نفسها نقصان العقل أو الهلاك، أو المرض سواء كان على نفسها أو ولدها نسباً كان أو رضاعاً ولها شرب الدواء إذا أخبر الطبيب أنه يمنع استطلاق بطن الرضيع، وتفطر لهذا العذر لقوله : إن الله وضع عن المسافر الصوم وشطر الصلاة وعن الحبلى والمرضع الصوم، ومن قيد بالمستأجرة للأرضاع فهو مردود قوله: (ومرضع) التي شأنها الإرضاع فتسمى به ولو في غير حال المباشرة، والمرضعة التي هي في حال الإرضاع ملقمة ثديها الصبي ذكره صاحب الكشاف قوله : (خافت نقصان العقل) خاص بها، وأما خوف الهلاك والمرض فيتحقق فيها، وفي الولد. قوله: (نسباً كان، أو رضاعاً أما الظئر فلأنه واجب عليها بالعقد، ولو كان العقد في رمضان كما في البرجندي . خلافاً لما في صدر الشريعة من تقييد حل الإفطار بما إذا صدرت الإجارة قبل رمضان، وأما الأم فلو جوبه عليها ديانة مطلقاً وقضاء إذا كان الأب معسراً، أو كان الولد يرضع من غيرها، وأما إذا أكره على الإفطار بهلاك ابنه، فلا يجوز له لأن العذر في الإكراه جاء من فعل من ليس له الحق فلا يعذر لصيانة نفس غيره بخلاف الحامل، والمرضع كذا في البحر. قوله: (وتفطر لهذا العذر أعاده، وإن فهم مما تقدم ليستدل عليه، ويحتمل أنه راجع إلى ما قبله فقط وقوله لقوله الخ علة للمصنف قوله : (فهو مردود) بالحديث السابق، وبأن الإرضاع واجب على الأم ديانة لا سيما إذا كان الأب معسراً كذا في الشرح
And it is permissible for the pregnant and breastfeeding woman who fears for herself a deficiency of intellect, or destruction, or illness, whether concerning herself or her child, whether the child is related by lineage or by breastfeeding. And she may drink medicine if the doctor informs her that it will prevent diarrhea in the infant, and she breaks the fast for this excuse because of his saying: 'Indeed Allah has lifted from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman the fast.' And whoever restricts it to the hired wet nurse is refuted. Commentary: (He said: 'breastfeeding woman') one whose occupation is breastfeeding, so she is called by it even when not actually nursing, while the one currently nursing is called murdi'ah, as mentioned by al-Kashshaf. (He said: 'she fears deficiency of intellect') is specific to herself; as for fear of destruction and illness, it applies to her and the child. (He said: 'whether by lineage or breastfeeding') as for the wet nurse (zahr), it is obligatory upon her by contract, even if the contract was in Ramadan, as in al-Birjandi, contrary to what is in Sadr al-Shari'ah which restricts the permissibility to if the contract was before Ramadan. As for the mother, it is obligatory upon her in principle and as a make-up if the father is insolvent or if the child nurses from no one else. As for one who is coerced to break fast by the destruction of his son, it is not permissible for him because the excuse of coercion comes from the act of one who has no right, so he is not excused to preserve another's life, unlike the pregnant and breastfeeding woman. (He said: 'and she breaks the fast for this excuse') he repeats it, even though it could be understood from what precedes, to indicate evidence. (He said: 'it is refuted') by the previous hadith, and because breastfeeding is obligatory upon the mother in principle, especially if the father is insolvent, as in the sharh.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn, supported by a hadith (dalil naqli). It states that a pregnant or breastfeeding woman who fears harm to herself—such as loss of intellect, destruction, or illness—or harm to her child (whether biological or through breastfeeding) may break the fast. The commentary clarifies that the fear of harm applies both to the woman and the child. A specific concession is mentioned: she may take medicine if a doctor advises it, and break the fast for that excuse. The proof is the hadith: 'Allah has lifted from the traveler... and from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman the fast.' This is a well-known hadith in the Sunan. The commentary also addresses a dispute: some restrict this permission to a hired wet nurse (musta'jirah), but the text declares this view refuted (mardood). The reasoning is that breastfeeding is an obligation upon the mother in principle, especially when the father is unable to provide a wet nurse. The hashiya also distinguishes between a coerced person (who cannot break fast for another's child) and the pregnant/breastfeeding woman, because the latter's excuse stems from a natural duty.

Key Terms
حامل (ḥāmil): A pregnant woman (with fetus in womb).
مرضع (murḍi'): A woman whose occupation or state is breastfeeding, includes both actual nursing and potential.
ظئر (ẓa'r): A wet nurse hired to breastfeed a child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited in the text for the permissibility of pregnant and breastfeeding women to break the fast?
A) A Quranic verse
B) A hadith stating Allah lifted fasting from them
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Qiyas on the sick

Key Takeaway
Pregnant and breastfeeding women may break the fast if they fear harm to themselves or their child, based on the general hadith; the concession is not limited to hired wet nurses.

Valid Grounds for Fear to Break Fast
Defines valid fear sources for fast-breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two valid sources of fear (khawf) that permit breaking the fast and identify the conditions required for each source.

Core Idea
Fear that permits breaking the fast must be based on predominant probability from experience or a reliable Muslim doctor's report — mere suspicion is not enough.

In the Hanafi school, breaking the fast is only permitted when there is a genuine fear of harm. This lesson clarifies the two recognized bases for such fear: prior experience (personal or analogous) and a qualified doctor's report. The commentary from al-Tahtawi refines the conditions, especially the requirement that the doctor be Muslim, skilled, and just, though the latter is debated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources
Read the passage and note the two means (ṭarīqān) of knowing valid fear: (1) predominant probability stemming from prior experience, (2) the report of a Muslim, skilled, just physician.
2
Understand ghalabat al-zann
Ghalabat al-zann is treated like certainty for concessions. It can arise from personal experience, experience of others for the same disease, or from signs interpreted through personal ijtihad. Mere suspicion (wahm) is insufficient.
The source equates predominant probability with certainty for this ruling.
3
Analyze the doctor's conditions
The doctor must be Muslim (to avoid potential sabotage), skilled (ḥādhiq), and just ('adl). However, the condition of 'adl is weakened by later authorities. Also, consulting a non-Muslim doctor is permissible when the act of worship is not at stake.
4
Synthesize the ruling
Combine all conditions: fear must be grounded in one of the two sources, and the doctor's report must come from a Muslim skilled physician. The requirement of justice is disputed but not necessary for the validity of the report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخوف المعتبر لإباحة الفطر طريق معرفته أمران أحدهما ما كان مستنداً فيه لغلبة الظن فإنها بمنزلة اليقين بتجربة سابقة، والثانية قوله أو إخبار طبيب مسلم حاذق عدل بداء كذا في البرهان، وقال الكمال : مسلم حاذق غير ظاهر الفسق وقيل : عدالته شرط قوله: (بتجربة) ولو كانت من غير المريض عند اتحاد المرض ذكره السيد في الحاشية، وزاد في البحر غلبة الظن الصادرة بأمارة ظهرت له باجتهاد والاجتهاد غير مجرد الوهم ا هـ. قوله : (مسلم) جرى على التقييد بالإسلام في الظهيرية حيث قال : وهو عندي محمول على المسلم دون الكافر كمسلم شرع في الصلاة بالتيمم فوعده كافر بالماء لا يقطع فلعل غرضه إفساد الصلاة عليه، فكذا في الصوم، وفيه إيماء إلى أنه يجوز أن يستطب بالكافر فيما ليس فيه إبطال عبادة ونهر. قوله : (حادق) أي له معرفة تامة في الطب فلا يجوز تقليد من له أدنى معرفة فيه . قوله : (عدل) جزم باشتراط العدالة الزيلعي ، وظاهر ما في البحر والنهر كالفتح ضعفه.
The fear considered for permitting breaking the fast: the means of knowing it are two things: one of them is based on predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann), for it is treated like certainty if based on prior experience; the second is the saying or report of a Muslim, skilled, just physician about a disease, as in al-Burhan. And al-Kamal said: a Muslim, skilled, not apparently sinful, and it was said: his integrity is a condition. Commentary: (He said: 'by experience') even if from someone other than the patient, when the disease is the same, as al-Sayyid mentioned in the hashiya. And al-Bahr added: predominant probability from a sign that appears to him by his own judgment (ijtihad), and ijtihad is not mere suspicion. (He said: 'Muslim') he followed the restriction to Islam in al-Dhahiriyyah, where he said: in my view it applies to a Muslim, not a disbeliever, like the case of a Muslim who begins prayer with tayammum, then a disbeliever promises him water; that promise does not break his prayer, perhaps because his intention is to spoil his prayer, similarly in fasting. And there is an indication that it is permissible to consult a disbelieving doctor in matters that do not involve nullification of worship. (He said: 'skilled') meaning he has complete knowledge of medicine, so one should not follow someone with minimal knowledge. (He said: 'just') al-Zayla'i affirmed the condition of integrity, but the apparent view in al-Bahr and al-Nahr, like al-Fat'h, is to weaken it.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage defines the valid grounds for fear (khawf) that permit breaking the fast. It is a definitional ruling specifying two authoritative sources for fear: first, predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann) based on prior personal or similar experience; second, the report of a Muslim doctor who is skilled (haadhiq) and just ('adl). The commentary expands on each condition. Experience can be from someone else if the disease is the same. Predominant probability can also arise from signs interpreted through personal ijtihad. The doctor must be Muslim, because a non-Muslim might spoil the act of worship; however, in medical matters not involving nullification of worship, consulting a non-Muslim doctor is permissible. The condition of the doctor being just is affirmed by al-Zayla'i but weakened by later scholars. The passage is critical in establishing that mere suspicion (wahm) is insufficient for breaking the fast; it must be based on reliable indicators.

Key Terms
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Predominant probability accepted as equivalent to certainty for legal permission, based on experience or expert information.
طبيب مسلم حاذق عدل (ṭabīb muslim ḥādhiq 'adl): A Muslim doctor who is skilled in medicine and just (upright). The condition of justice is disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must the fear (khawf) be based on to permit breaking the fast?
A) Any doubt or suspicion
B) A general feeling of discomfort
C) Previous experience or a Muslim skilled doctor's report
D) The advice of any medical professional

Key Takeaway
Valid fear that permits breaking the fast must be grounded in predominant probability from experience or a qualified Muslim doctor's report; mere suspicion is insufficient.

Ruling on Breaking Fast Due to Extreme Thirst or Hunger
Ruling on breaking fast due to extreme thirst/hunger with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which breaking fast is permitted due to extreme thirst or hunger, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on kaffarah when the condition is self-inflicted.

Core Idea
Extreme thirst or hunger threatening life or senses permits breaking the fast; kaffarah may apply if self-inflicted.

Fasting in Ramadan is a fundamental obligation, but the Shari'a prioritizes preserving life and health. This lesson addresses a critical exception: when extreme thirst or hunger poses a real risk of death, mental impairment, or loss of senses. We will examine the conditions that make breaking the fast permissible and the nuance regarding kaffarah when the condition is self-caused.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its key conditions
The matn states that breaking the fast is permitted for one who experiences extreme thirst or hunger that leads to fear of death, loss of intellect, or senses, provided it is not self-inflicted.
The condition of 'not self-inflicted' relates to kaffarah, not permissibility.
2
Distinguish between permissibility and kaffarah
The permissibility to break is unconditional for genuine emergencies; the condition about self-infliction only affects whether kaffarah is required. The commentary clarifies this.
Some scholars hold that kaffarah is obligatory if the condition was caused by one's own actions.
3
Apply the examples from the commentary
Al-Qahastani's examples include laborers, slaves, and even domestic workers facing extreme heat. Understand that the excuse is based on harm, not social status.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a disagreement on whether kaffarah is obligatory when the extreme condition results from self-exertion. The text leaves this unresolved.
Both positions are legitimate within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجاز الفطر لمن حصل له عطش شديد أو جوع مفرط يخاف منه الهلاك أو نقصان العقل، أو ذهاب بعض الحواس وكان ذلك لا بإتعاب نفسه إذ لو كان به تلزمه الكفارة وقيل : لا قوله: (يخاف منه الهلاك) ذكر القهستاني عن الخزانة ما نصه أن الحر الخادم أو العبد أو الذاهب لسد النهر، أو كريه إذا اشتد الحر، وخاف الهلاك فله الإفطار كحرة، أو أمة ضعفت للطبخ، أو غسل الثوب ا هـ . قوله : (وكان ذلك الخ) الظاهر أن القيد لإسقاط الكفارة أما حل الفطر للأعذار المذكورة، فالظاهر الجواز مطلقاً كما تدل عليه عبارة القهستاني
And it is permitted to break the fast for one who experiences extreme thirst or excessive hunger from which he fears destruction, or deficiency of intellect, or loss of some senses, and that was not due to his exhausting himself, for if it was due to him, kaffarah would be obligatory. And it is said: no. Commentary: (He said: 'fears destruction from it') Al-Qahastani mentioned from al-Khizanah the text: the free servant, or the slave, or the one going to repair the riverbank, or his canal, if the heat intensifies and he fears destruction, he may break the fast, like a free woman, or a female slave who is weak from cooking or washing clothes. (He said: 'and that was etc.') The apparent meaning is that the condition is to waive kaffarah; as for the permissibility of breaking the fast for the mentioned excuses, the apparent ruling is permissibility absolutely, as indicated by the expression of Al-Qahastani.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage rules on extreme thirst or hunger as a valid excuse to break the fast. The matn states that if one fears death, loss of intellect, or loss of senses from severe thirst/hunger, breaking the fast is permitted, provided the condition was not self-inflicted (i.e., by overexertion). If it was self-inflicted, kaffarah may be obligatory. The commentary clarifies that the condition pertains to kaffarah, not to the permissibility itself; the permissibility is absolute for genuine emergencies. Al-Qahastani's examples include workers in extreme heat, slaves, and even those performing household chores like cooking or washing. This reflects the Hanafi concern for preventing harm (darar) and preserving human well-being. The dispute concerning kaffarah is noted: some say kaffarah is obligatory if the condition was self-inflicted; others say no.

Key Terms
عطش شديد / جوع مفرط (ʿaṭash shadīd / jūʿ mufriṭ): Extreme thirst or excessive hunger that poses a risk of death, loss of intellect, or senses.
إتعاب نفسه (itʿāb nafsihī): Exhausting oneself, e.g., through excessive labor, which leads to the extreme condition.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation required for invalidating a fast without valid excuse; in this context, disputed for self-inflicted extreme conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is still permissible without kaffarah
B) Kaffarah may be obligatory
C) The fast is invalid and must be repeated
D) No consequence

Key Takeaway
Breaking fast due to extreme thirst/hunger threatening life or senses is permissible; the dispute on kaffarah for self-inflicted cases remains.

Ruling on Traveler Breaking Fast
States permissibility for traveler to break fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a traveler breaking fast and apply the principle that sinfulness does not negate the travel concession.

Core Idea
A traveler may break fast even on a sinful journey – sin does not remove the legal concession.

This lesson addresses the permissibility for a traveler to break the fast during Ramadan. The Hanafi school holds that the key is the legal nature of the journey, not the moral purpose. Even if the travel is for disobedience, the concession remains valid. This is based on a principle that an incidental sin does not invalidate the legal validity of the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is breaking the fast while traveling. The ruling is mubah (permissible) in the Hanafi school.
2
Define the condition
The travel must be legally recognized (safar shar'i), meaning a journey in which prayer can be shortened (qasr). Hanafis define this by distance (approx. 48 miles) or three days' travel.
This condition is crucial for the concession to apply.
3
Apply the exclusion for sinful purpose
Even if the journey is for disobedience, the permissibility to break fast remains. The sinfulness of the purpose does not negate the legal nature of the travel.
The principle: 'al-qubh al-mujawir la yudim al-mashru'iyyah' (adjacent ugliness does not negate legal validity).
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools restrict travel concessions to permissible journeys. In Hanafi, the concession applies regardless of the journey's purpose as long as it meets the distance condition.
This ikhtilaf is a legitimate scholarly difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمسافر أي سفراً شرعياً وهو الذي تقصر فيه الصلاة، ولو لمعصية لأن القبح المجاور لا يعدم المشروعية، بحر
And for the traveler: i.e., a legally recognized travel which is the one in which prayer is shortened, even if for disobedience, because the adjacent ugliness does not negate legal validity, as in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This is a brief ruling on the traveler's permission to break the fast. The hashiya clarifies that the travel must be of the type in which prayer is shortened (qasr), which is a journey of approximately 48 miles or three days. Interestingly, the text notes that even a journey for a sinful purpose qualifies, because the sinfulness of the purpose does not negate the legal concession of traveling itself. This is a principle: the ruling of travel concessions applies regardless of the sinfulness of the journey, as long as it is legally considered travel. The source is al-Bahr al-Raiq.

Key Terms
سفر شرعي (safar sharʿī): A journey that qualifies for legal concessions, such as shortening prayer (qasr) and breaking fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because the journey is sinful
B) Yes, because the sin does not negate the legal concession
C) Only if the journey is long
D) Only if the traveler repents

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a traveler on a legally recognized journey to break the fast, regardless of the sinfulness of the journey's purpose.

Ruling for Pregnant and Breastfeeding Woman Breaking Fast
Explains when and why a pregnant or nursing woman may break her fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a pregnant or breastfeeding woman may break her fast in Ramadan, cite the hadith proof, and distinguish the two accepted methods of establishing legitimate fear.

Core Idea
A pregnant or nursing woman may break her fast if she genuinely fears harm—based on prior experience or a reliable doctor's report.

Fasting in Ramadan is obligatory, but Allah has granted exemptions for those with valid excuses. Among them are pregnant and breastfeeding women who fear harm to themselves or their child. This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling on when such women may break their fast and the evidentiary basis from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The default ruling for a pregnant or breastfeeding woman who fears harm is that it is permissible to break the fast. This is a concession (rukhsah) from the original obligation. If the fear is severe, it may become obligatory.
2
Examine the hadith proof
The text cites the hadith: 'Allah has lifted fasting and half the prayer from the traveler, and fasting from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman.' This directly permits breaking the fast for these women.
The hadith is reported in Sunan Abi Dawud.
3
Understand the source of fear
Two methods are accepted to establish the fear: (a) overwhelming probability (ghalabat al-ẓann) from prior personal experience, and (b) the report of a qualified Muslim doctor. Ghalabat al-ẓann is considered like certainty in this context.
4
Verify the doctor's qualifications
The doctor must be Muslim, skilled (ḥādhiq), and just (‘adl). Al-Kamal adds that he should not be openly sinful. The doctor's report must be about a specific illness or harm.
A beginner or a non-Muslim is not acceptable.
5
Reject the restrictive view
Some earlier scholars restricted this permission to a hired wet nurse only. The text explicitly rejects this restriction, affirming that it applies to any nursing mother, whether biological or through radā‘.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولدها نسباً كان أو رضاعاً ولها شرب الدواء إذا أخبر الطبيب أنه يمنع استطلاق بطن الرضيع، وتفطر لهذا العذر لقوله : إن الله وضع عن المسافر الصوم وشطر الصلاة وعن الحبلى والمرضع الصوم، ومن قيد بالمستأجرة للأرضاع فهو مردود (والخوف المعتبر) لإباحة الفطر طريق معرفته أمران أحدهما ما كان (مستنداً) فيه (لغلبة الظن فإنها بمنزلة اليقين (بتجربة سابقة، والثانية قوله أو إخبار طبيب) مسلم حاذق عدل بداء كذا في البرهان، وقال الكمال : مسلم حاذق غير ظاهر الفسق وقيل : عدالته شرط
Her child, whether by lineage or breastfeeding; and she may drink medicine if a doctor informs her that it prevents diarrhea of the infant, and she breaks her fast for this excuse due to his saying: 'Indeed Allah has lifted from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman the fast.' And whoever restricts it to the hired wet nurse is rejected. (And the fear considered) for permitting breaking the fast: the way of knowing it is two matters: one: that it be based on overwhelming probability, and it is like certainty, by prior experience; and the second: his saying or a doctor's report: a Muslim, skilled, just, about an illness. Thus in al-Burhan. And al-Kamal said: Muslim, skilled, not openly sinful; and it was said: his justice is a condition.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the pregnant and breastfeeding woman. It first clarifies that the child in question can be either biological or through breastfeeding (radā‘). The woman is permitted to take medicine if a trustworthy doctor advises it to prevent harm to the infant, and she may break her fast for this reason. The proof is the hadith: 'Allah has lifted fasting and half the prayer from the traveler, and fasting from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman.' Any opinion restricting this to a hired wet nurse only is rejected. The passage then discusses the conditions for legitimate fear that permits breaking the fast. There are two ways to establish this fear: (1) overwhelming probability (ghalabat al-ẓann) based on prior personal experience, or (2) the report of a qualified doctor. The doctor must be Muslim, skilled (ḥādhiq), and just (‘adl). Al-Kamal adds that the doctor should not be openly sinful. Some require explicit justice. This follows the Hanafi position that fear, even if only probable, suffices when based on sound indicators.

Key Terms
الحامل (al-ḥāmil): A pregnant woman
المرضع (al-murḍi‘): A breastfeeding woman
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-ẓann): An overwhelming probability tantamount to certainty
حادق (ḥādhiq): Skilled, having deep knowledge of medicine

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two methods are accepted to establish the fear that allows a pregnant or breastfeeding woman to break her fast?
A) Certainty based on a dream and a doctor's report
B) Overwhelming probability from prior experience or a report from a skilled Muslim doctor
C) A doctor's report even if he is a non-Muslim
D) The woman's own feeling without any external evidence

Key Takeaway
A pregnant or breastfeeding woman may break her fast if she genuinely fears harm, either from prior experience or a reliable Muslim doctor's report.

Breaking Fast for Severe Thirst or Hunger
States the ruling when fasting causes fear of death or loss of senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when it is permissible to break a fast due to thirst or hunger and identify whether kaffarah is required based on the cause of the condition.

Core Idea
Severe thirst or hunger permits breaking fast, but kaffarah may still be due if self-inflicted.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: when a fasting person faces intense thirst or hunger that leads to fear of death or impairment, breaking the fast becomes permissible. However, the school distinguishes between conditions arising from external factors and those caused by one's own exertion. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct application and fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is breaking the fast (futūr) due to severe thirst or hunger. The ruling is permissibility (jawāz). Determine when this permission activates.
The permission is contingent on fear of halāk, naqṣān al-‘aql, or dhahāb ba‘ḍ al-ḥawās.
2
Distinguish the cause of the condition
Check whether the severe thirst or hunger resulted from the person's own exertion (it‘āb nafsihī) or from external factors. This distinction affects kaffārah.
If from self-exertion, kaffārah is obligatory; if not, no kaffārah.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some scholars (qīla lā) hold that kaffārah is not required even if self-exertion caused the fear. Understand both positions.
The relied-upon view (muftā bihī) is that kaffārah is due when the condition is self-inflicted.
4
Apply the qiyās reasoning
The majority analogize to deliberate breaking of the fast: the effective cause ('illah) is the intentional action leading to the necessity of breaking.
Qiyās applies when the cause is deliberate, even if the immediate excuse is genuine.
5
Summarize the practical outcome
If a fasting person breaks due to severe thirst/hunger: (a) if from external causes → permissible, no kaffārah; (b) if from self-exertion → permissible, but kaffārah required.
The ruling is limited to the person experiencing the danger; others must continue fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) جاز الفطر لمن حصل له عطش شديد أو جوع مفرط يخاف منه الهلاك) أو نقصان العقل، أو ذهاب بعض الحواس وكان ذلك لا بإتعاب نفسه إذ لو كان به تلزمه الكفارة وقيل : لا (وللمسافر)
And it is permissible to break the fast for one who experiences intense thirst or excessive hunger from which he fears destruction, or loss of intellect, or loss of some senses, and that (the condition) is not from his own exertion; for if it were, the kaffarah would be binding upon him. And it is said: no. (And for the traveler).
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on breaking fast due to severe thirst or hunger. The criterion is fear of death (halāk), loss of intellect (naqṣān al-‘aql), or loss of some senses (dhahāb ba‘ḍ al-ḥawās). A key condition: this fear must not arise from one's own exertion (i.e., not from deliberately exhausting oneself in work or exercise). If it does, then breaking the fast still incurs kaffārah (expiation). There is a difference of opinion: some say if the fear is from self-exertion, breaking fast is still allowed but kaffārah is due; others say it is not allowed. The text leans toward the first view. This ruling is specific to the person experiencing the danger; it does not apply to others. The phrase 'wal-musāfir' indicates that the next topic is the traveler, but it is likely a heading.

Key Terms
العطش الشديد (al-‘aṭash al-shadīd): Intense thirst that leads to fear of death
نقصان العقل (naqṣān al-‘aql): Loss of intellect/insanity
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation for breaking fast: freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor
إتعاب النفس (it‘āb al-nafs): Self-exertion, deliberately tiring oneself

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must continue fasting
B) He may break his fast but must pay kaffarah
C) He may break without any penalty
D) It is haram to break

Key Takeaway
Breaking fast is permissible due to severe thirst/hunger leading to fear of harm, but kaffarah is obligatory when the condition is self-inflicted.

Traveler's Fasting: When to Fast and When to Break
Ruling on traveler's fasting with Quranic proofs and social adjustment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the traveler's fasting preference and identify the social circumstances that change the recommendation from fasting to breaking.

Core Idea
Fasting is better for the traveler — unless your companions break fast or share costs, then breaking is best.

This lesson examines the delicate balance between individual devotion and group harmony in the traveler's fasting. While the Quran grants the traveler the option to break fast, the preferred course changes based on the practical dynamics of the journey. We will explore the evidence and the social factors that tip the scale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling and its category
Determine whether fasting or breaking is the default preference. The text states that fasting is 'more beloved' (aḥabb) unless certain conditions exist.
The default is that fasting is recommended (mustaḥabb) for the traveler.
2
Locate the dalil from Quran and hadith
Note the verse (Al-Baqarah 2:184) that grants permission to break, and the verse (2:185) that encourages fasting. Also consider the hadith alluded to by 'wa mā rawaināhu'.
The Quranic concession makes breaking permissible, while the encouragement shows preference.
3
Determine the condition under which fasting is loved
The text says fasting is loved 'if it does not harm him.' This means if fasting causes significant hardship, breaking becomes more appropriate.
Harm includes excessive thirst, weakness, or any risk to health.
4
Identify the social exceptions that override the default
Two scenarios override the default: (1) the majority of companions are breaking fast, or (2) the group shares expenses. In these cases, breaking becomes afḍal (better) to maintain social harmony (muwāfaqat al-jamā‘ah).
This reflects the Hanafi sensitivity to social context in acts of worship.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The choice is not absolute but contingent. A traveler who would normally fast should break if it leads to better group cohesion or prevents resentment.
This ruling differs from the Shāfi‘ī school, which holds fasting always better unless hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمسافر الذي أنشأ السفر قبل طلوع الفجر إذ لا يباح له الفطر بإنشائه بعدما أصبح صائماً بخلاف ما لو حل به مرض بعده فله (الفطر) لقوله تعالى : فمن كان منكم مريضاً أو على سفر فعدة من أيام أخر ولما رويناه (وصومه) أي المسافر (أحب إن لم يضره) لقوله تعالى: (وأن تصوموا خير لكم ) (و) هذا إذا لم تكن عامة رفقته مفطرين، ولا مشتركين في النفقة، فإن كانوا مشتركين، أو مفطرين فالأفضل فطره) أي المسافر (موافقة للجماعة) كما في الجوهرة
And for the traveler who initiated the journey before dawn, for it is not permissible for him to break his fast by initiating it (the journey) after he has become fasting, unlike if an illness befalls him after that; then he may break. Due to His statement: 'So whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then an equal number of other days.' And due to what we have narrated. And his fasting (i.e., the traveler's) is more beloved if it does not harm him, due to His statement: 'And that you fast is better for you.' And this is when the majority of his companions are not breaking fast, and they are not sharing expenses. If they are sharing expenses, or are breaking fast, then the best for him (the traveler) is to break fast in agreement with the group, as in al-Jawharah.
Source pages p. 685

Commentary
This passage covers the traveler's fasting. It first clarifies that if a person begins fasting at home and then travels after dawn, he cannot break his fast due to the travel itself; but if illness occurs after travel, he may break. The primary proof is from the Quran (Al-Baqarah 184) and the hadith previously mentioned. The general ruling is that the traveler has a choice: fasting is afḍal (better) if it does not cause harm, as the Quran says 'and that you fast is better for you.' However, this preference changes based on circumstances. If the majority of the traveler's companions are not fasting, or if the group shares expenses (common food), then breaking the fast becomes afḍal to maintain harmony and avoid hardship. The source 'al-Jawharah' is cited. This shows the Hanafi emphasis on social considerations (muwāfaqat al-jamā‘ah) even in individual acts of worship.

Key Terms
المسافر (al-musāfir): One traveling a shar‘ī distance (shortening prayers)
الرفقة (al-rafaqah): Traveling companions
الأفضل (al-afḍal): The better option, not obligatory
مشتركين (mushtarikīn): Those sharing expenses (e.g., common food provisions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The traveler's fasting preference is not absolute; social context can shift the recommended action from fasting to breaking.

Bequest for Missed Fasts: When Is It Obligatory?
Explains when bequest for missed fasts is required and the stratagem for payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a bequest for missed fasts is wajib in Hanafi fiqh and apply the legal stratagem for payment.

Core Idea
Bequest for missed fasts only binds if the deceased could have made them up but did not.

This passage clarifies a key fiqh ruling about the obligations of a deceased's missed fasts. It distinguishes between those who die while still excused (e.g., ill) and those who die after the excuse ended but did not make up. The Hanafi school analogizes missed fasts to debts that must be settled when the opportunity existed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the bequest (īṣā’) for missed fasts. The ruling is that it becomes wajib on the heirs under specific conditions.
2
Check the condition for obligation
Determine if the deceased had the opportunity to make up the missed fasts before death. If the excuse ended and he was able but did not, bequest is required. If he died while still excused, no bequest.
Opportunity (zawāl al-ʿudhr) is the key condition.
3
Analyze the dalil (qiyas)
The ruling is based on qiyas: missed fasts with opportunity are like debts that must be settled after death.
The Quranic command to make up missed fasts (2:184) implies the obligation remains if able.
4
Understand the legal stratagem (ḥīlah)
If the deceased left insufficient assets, the heirs may borrow half a ṣāʿ of grain, give it to a poor person, and have it returned, repeating for each missed day. This fulfills the fidyah.
This permissibility is specific to the Hanafi school.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools (e.g., Shafiʿi) hold that missed fasts lapse at death; Hanafis require fidyah if opportunity existed.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجب الإيصاء بكفارة ما أفطره على من مات قبل زوال عذره) بمرض وسفر ونحوه كما تقدم من الأعذار المبيحة للفطر لفوات إدراك عدة من أيام أخر (و) إن أدرك العدة (قضوا ما قدروا على قضائه وإن لم يقضوا لزمهم الإيصاء بقدر الإقامة) من السفر
And it is not obligatory to bequeath kaffarah for what he broke fast upon one who died before his excuse ceased, due to illness, travel, and the like, as previously mentioned among the excuses that permit breaking the fast, due to the failure to reach (the opportunity to make up) a number of other days. And if he did reach the (ability to make up) days, (the heirs) will make up what they are able to, and if they do not make up, bequest is binding upon them to the extent of (the missed days of) residency from travel.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage deals with the obligation of bequest (īṣā’) for missed fasts of a deceased person. The general rule: if a person dies while his excuse (like illness or travel) still exists, he has not had the opportunity to make up the fasts, so no bequest is necessary. The reason (ʿillah) is that the 'number of other days' (ʿiddah min ayyām ukhar) was still within reach, but he died before the excuse lifted. However, if the excuse ended (e.g., he recovered or returned from travel) and he had the ability to make up but did not, then the heirs must make up the fasts if possible. If they are unable to fast (e.g., due to death), they must bequeath (provide fidyah) from his estate for the missed days. The phrase 'qadr al-iqāmah' refers to the days he spent in residency after travel. The commentary further discusses that prohibited days (like Eid) are excluded because one cannot make up on those days. Also, a practical solution (ḥīlah) is provided for paying fidyah when the deceased had no assets: borrow half a ṣā‘ and give it to a poor person, who then gives it back, repeating until all missed days are covered.

Key Terms
الإيصاء (al-īṣā’): Bequest to pay fidyah for missed fasts
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Fidyah of feeding a poor person per missed day
الحيلة (al-ḥīlah): Legal stratagem to fulfill obligation without wealth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When he was financially poor
B) When he died while his excuse (like illness) still existed
C) When his heirs refuse to pay
D) When he missed only one day

Key Takeaway
Bequest for missed fasts is only obligatory on heirs if the deceased had the opportunity to make them up but failed to do so.

Ruling on Fasting While Traveling: The Hanafi Position
Critical commentary on the traveler's fast ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting while traveling, identify the conditions under which breaking the fast is preferred or obligatory, and explain the evidentiary reasoning from Quran and Hadith.

Core Idea
Fasting in travel is the stronger resolve ('azima) unless harm; then breaking becomes obligatory.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on fasting while traveling, a topic where the interplay of Quranic concession, hadith, and scholarly commentary yields a nuanced position. The primary source clarifies that fasting is the recommended 'azima for a traveler who is not harmed, but breaking becomes better when most companions break or when expenses are shared. The lesson also addresses the apparent conflict with a hadith that seems to discourage fasting in travel, demonstrating how Hanafi jurists harmonize texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Determine the basic ruling for a traveler who begins fasting before travel: is it permissible to break? The text says that if he initiated travel after dawn, he may not break, but if illness occurs, he may.
Distinguish between initiating travel while fasting vs. illness supervening.
2
Locate the central proofs
Two Quranic verses (2:184) and a hadith are cited: one gives permission to break for the traveler, the other declares fasting 'better', and the hadith lifts the fast obligation. Identify how each supports the ruling.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'azima and rukhsa.
3
Analyze the conditions affecting preference
The preference for fasting is conditioned on: (1) no harm from fasting, (2) most companions are not breaking fast, and (3) expenses are not shared. If harm is feared, breaking becomes wajib. If most companions break or expenses are shared, breaking is better.
Note the refinement: 'majority' (ghalaba) vs. few; shared expenses create financial harm analogy.
4
Trace the corrective interpretation of opposing hadith
The hadith 'There is no righteousness in fasting while traveling' is explained as applicable only when fasting causes harm. The commentator resolves the apparent conflict by interpreting 'khayr' in the verse as 'birr' (righteousness) rather than comparative superiority.
This critical refinement shows how Hanafi scholars reconcile seemingly contradictory texts.
5
Synthesize the final ruling
Combine the elements: fasting is better for a traveler who is not harmed, not in a situation where breaking is socially or financially preferable. Otherwise, break is better; if harm threatens, break is obligatory.
The mufta bihi: fasting is sunnah muakkadah unless harm, then wajib to break.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم الذي أنشأ السفر قبل طلوع الفجر إذ لا يباح له الفطر بإنشائه بعدما أصبح صائماً بخلاف ما لو حل به مرض بعده فله (الفطر) لقوله تعالى : فمن كان منكم مريضاً أو على سفر فعدة من أيام أخر ولما رويناه (وصومه) أي المسافر (أحب إن لم يضره) لقوله تعالى: (وأن تصوموا خير لكم ) (و) هذا إذا لم تكن عامة رفقته مفطرين، ولا مشتركين في النفقة، فإن كانوا مشتركين، أو مفطرين فالأفضل فطره) أي المسافر (موافقة للجماعة) كما في الجوهرة. وأشار باللام إلى أنه مخير بين الصوم والفطر لكن الفطر رخصة والصوم عزيمة فكان أفضل إلا إذا خاف الهلاك، فالإفطار واجب كما في البحر. قوله : (إذ لا يباح له الفطر بإنشائه الخ) لكن إذا أفطر لا كفارة عليه قاله السيد وقد تقدم قوله: (فعدة من أيام أخر) أي فأفطر فعليه عدة الأيام التي أفطرها من أيام أخر قوله : (ولما رويناه) أي من قوله : إن الله وضع عن المسافر الصوم. قوله : (إن لم يضره ) أراد بالضرر الضرر الذي ليس فيه خوف الهلاك لأن ما فيه خوف الهلاك بسبب الصوم، فالإفطار في مثله واجب لا أنه أفضل بحر. قوله: (لقوله تعالى، وأن تصوموا خير لكم ولأن رمضان أفضل أفضل فكان الأداء أفضل، وأما قوله : ليس من البر الصيام في السفر فمحمول على مسافر ضره الصوم زيلعي قال في الدرر والخير بمعنى البر لا أفعل تفضيل أي لاقتضائه أن الإفطار فيه خير مع أنه مباح، وفيه نظر ذكرته في حاشية الدر. قوله: (وهذا إذا لم تكن عامة رفقته مفطرين قيد بالعامة فأفاد أن القليل لو أفطر لا يكون الفطر أفضل قوله : (فإن كانوا مشتركين أي وأفطروا أي وإن لم يكونوا عامتهم، وقيد المسئلة في الدر بمشقة إفطاره على رفقته. قوله : (أو مفطرين) أي وإن لم يكونوا مشتركين في النفقة. قوله : (موافقة للجماعة) عدل إليه عن قول صاحب البحر إذا كانت النفقة مشتركة، فالفطر أفضل لما أن ضرر المال كضرر النفس لما قاله في النهر إن التعليل بموافقة الجماعة أولى، وأما لزوم ضرر المال بضياعه بصومه، فممنوع أفاده في تحفة الأخيار أي لجواز أن يأخذ نصيبه، ويبقيه، أو يكون سمحاً يتجاوز عن نصيبه.
The Book of Fasting. One who began travel before dawn: it is not permitted for him to break his fast by initiating travel after he has become a faster, contrary to if an illness befalls him after (he started fasting), for then he may break his fast, due to His saying: 'Whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days' and due to what we have narrated. And his fast (i.e., the traveler) is more beloved if it does not harm him, due to His saying: 'And that you fast is better for you.' This is when not most of his traveling companions are breaking the fast, nor are they partners in expenses. If they are partners (in expenses) or are breaking the fast, then the better for him (the traveler) is to break his fast, in conformity with the group, as in al-Jawhara. And he (al‑Lām) indicated that he is given a choice between fasting and breaking, but breaking is a concession and fasting is a resolve, so it is better, except if he fears perishing, then breaking is obligatory, as in al-Bahr. His saying: 'For it is not permitted for him to break by initiating it, etc.': but if he breaks, there is no expiation upon him, said al‑Sayyid, and it has preceded. His saying: 'a number of other days' i.e., he breaks, so he owes a number of days equal to the days he broke. His saying: 'and what we narrated' i.e., from his saying: 'Allah has lifted from the traveler the fast.' His saying: 'if it does not harm him': he intended by 'harm' harm that does not involve fear of perishing, for what involves fear of perishing due to fasting, then breaking in such a case is obligatory, not that it is better, (as in) Bahr. His saying: 'due to His saying: And that you fast is better for you, and because Ramadan is better, so performance (of the fast) is better.' As for his saying: 'There is no righteousness in fasting while traveling,' it is interpreted as referring to a traveler whom fasting harms, as al‑Zayla‘i said. In al‑Durar, 'good' (khayr) has the meaning of righteousness (birr), not superiority, because it would imply that breaking is good while it is permissible, and there is a critique which I mentioned in my gloss on al‑Dur. His saying: 'This is when not most of his traveling companions are breaking the fast': he qualified by 'most,' so it indicates that if a few break, breaking is not better. His saying: 'If they are partners (in expenses) and they break': i.e., even if they are not the majority, and he qualified the issue in al‑Dur by the difficulty of his breaking on his companions. His saying: 'or if they are breaking': i.e., even if they are not partners in expenses. His saying: 'in conformity with the group': he turned to this from the statement of the author of al‑Bahr: 'if expenses are shared, breaking is better because harm to property is like harm to oneself'; for what he said in al‑Nahr: 'the reasoning of conformity with the group is more appropriate.' As for the occurrence of harm to property by its wastage due to his fasting, it is rejected, as in Tuhfat al‑Akhyār: because it is possible that he takes his share and keeps it, or he is generous and overlooks his share.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on a ruling from Maraqi al-Falah regarding the traveler's fast. The matn states that if a person initiates travel after dawn, he may not break his fast because he began the day as a resident faster; however, if illness befalls him after starting the fast, he may break. The proof is the Quranic verse permitting break for the ill and traveler, and a hadith that Allah lifts the fast from the traveler. The matn also says that the traveler's fast is 'more beloved' (ahabb), i.e., recommended, if it does not harm him, citing the verse 'And that you fast is better for you.' The commentator clarifies that this recommendation applies when most of his traveling companions are not breaking fast and there is no shared expense arrangement. If the majority is breaking or expenses are shared, then breaking fast is better to avoid financial harm to the party. The commentator notes that the matn's wording indicates a choice, but fasting is the 'azima (resolution) and breaking is rukhsa (concession), so fasting is better unless there is fear of death, in which case breaking becomes obligatory. He also engages with a hadith that says 'there is no righteousness in fasting while traveling,' interpreting it as referring to a case where fasting causes harm. A detailed discussion follows about the meaning of 'khayr' (good) in the verse, concluding that it means 'birr' (righteousness) rather than a comparative superiority. The commentator also refines the condition about companions: the ruling is based on the majority, not the minority, and when expenses are shared, breaking is preferred to avoid financial loss, though this is qualified by the possibility that the fasting traveler may take his share and keep it.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): Travel that excuses one from fasting; in Hanafi fiqh, a journey of three days (approx. 48 miles) or more.
رخصة (rukhsa): A legal concession that allows the traveler to break the fast, based on the Quranic verse.
عزيمة (azima): The original ruling of obligation or recommendation without concession; here, fasting is the 'azima for the traveler who is not harmed.
ضرر (darar): Harm; if fasting causes harm (even without fear of death), breaking becomes better; if fear of perishing, breaking becomes obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does breaking the fast become obligatory (wajib) for a traveler?
A) When most companions are breaking fast.
B) When expenses are shared with companions.
C) When fasting causes fear of perishing (khawf al-halak).
D) Always, because it is a concession.

Key Takeaway
Fasting while traveling is the recommended 'azima unless harm is feared, when breaking becomes obligatory; social and financial conditions may also make breaking better.

Bequest for Missed Fasts and Consecutive Fasts
Explains the obligation of bequest for missed fasts and lists fasts requiring consecutive days.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequest for missed fasts (wasiyya), identify the fasts that require consecutive days (tatabu'), and apply the ruling to the case of a conditional vow (nadhr) with partial recovery.

Core Idea
Missed fasts require fidya bequest; conditional vows trigger full-month obligation upon any recovery.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies two interrelated rulings: the obligation of the deceased to arrange fidya (feeding) for missed fasts, and the classification of fasts that must be observed consecutively. It also resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a person who vowed to fast a month if he recovers from illness, but only recovers for a single day before dying. Understanding these rulings is essential for correctly discharging religious obligations after death and properly executing a will (wasiyya).

Guided Path
1
Establish the obligation of wasiyya for missed fasts
The passage begins by stating that missing fasts (fawāt) is the cause for the author's statement that qada is obligatory. From this, the commentator derives the benefit that the deceased must bequeath feeding (fidya). Identify the logical link: the obligation of qada transfers to a bequest when death prevents performance.
The term 'fawāt' is the legal cause (illah) for the obligation of wasiyya.
2
Identify the conditions for executing the wasiyya
The wasiyya is executed from one-third of the estate, provided there are no debts due to people. If there is a debt, the bequest is deferred to the remaining third after debt payment. Also, if the deceased left no property, the heir may borrow and give fidya as described.
Debt takes precedence over bequest.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the vowed month
The passage presents a dispute: a person vows to fast a month if he recovers, then recovers for only one day and dies. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the bequest covers the entire month; Muhammad holds only the days actually fasted. Compare the evidence (dalil) for each view and identify the mufta bihi position.
The difference hinges on whether recovery for any period activates the entire vow.
4
Classify fasts by requirement of consecutiveness
The passage lists four fasts that are explicitly consecutively required (by Quranic text) and three that are established by hadith. Understand that qada of Ramadan is not required to be consecutive, but it is recommended. This classification affects how a missed fast must be performed.
For vowed fasts, consecutiveness depends on the wording of the vow.
5
Apply the rulings to a practical case
Using the principles learned, construct a scenario where a person has missed fasts and a conditional vow, then died. Determine the fidya amount and how it is paid from the estate, considering debts.
This synthesis step integrates multiple rulings from the passage.

If he bequeathed but left no property, he should borrow half a sa' and give it to a poor person, then that poor person gives it back as charity or gift, and so on until each fast is completed with half a sa'. His saying: '(and compensation for game)' killed while in ihram or in the Haram. His saying: '(as for vowing consecutive days)': 'nadhara' has a kasra and damm on the dhal, as in al‑Qamus; the quadruple verb 'andhara' means to inform, warn, and frighten. His saying: 'or unspecified (days) in particular' means that the consideration is on mentioning consecutiveness, whether he specifies, like 'a month of Rajab consecutive', or does not specify, like 'a month consecutive'. If he breaks one day in the first, he makes it up without starting over, lest it all fall outside the time; in the second, he starts over because he violated the description, as in al‑Tanwir and its commentary on the obstacles to fasting.
