Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on several interrelated rulings: bequest for missed fasts, the requirement of consecutive days for certain fasts, and the effect of vows. The commentator first addresses the author's statement that qada (make-up) of missed fasts is obligatory. He explains that a person who missed fasts due to a valid excuse (travel/illness) and died before making them up must bequeath (wasiyya) that food be given for each missed day. This bequest is from the one-third of the estate, provided there are no debts to people; if there is a debt, it is from the remaining one-third after debt. If there is no property, the heir should borrow half a sa' and give it to a poor person, then the poor person gives it back as charity, repeating until each day is covered. Without a bequest, the heir is not obliged to fast for the deceased. Then the commentator clarifies that the agreement on the obligation of qada after removal of the excuse is unanimous, but a dispute arises when a person vowed to fast a month if he recovers from illness, then recovers for only one day: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, he must bequeath feeding for the entire month; according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, he only makes up the days he actually fasted. The commentator notes that consecutiveness in qada is not obligatory but recommended. Next, he lists fasts that must be consecutive: those explicitly mentioned in the Quran (Ramadan, kaffara for zihar, accidental killing, and oath-breaking) and those established by hadith (kaffara for deliberately breaking Ramadan fast, supererogatory fasts are optional, and vowed fasts depend on intention). He explains that if a new Ramadan arrives before qada is completed, the current Ramadan takes priority, and qada is postponed. The commentator also addresses the ruling on voluntary fasting on behalf of the deceased: it is valid for food (fidya) but not for freeing a slave due to wala', and prayer is like fasting by istihsan, but one may not pray on behalf of another.

Key Terms
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A bequest made by a dying person; here specifically for fidya (feeding for missed fasts).
التتابع (al-tatabu'): Consecutive days of fasting without interruption; required for certain categories of fasts as listed.
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship; here specifically a vow to fast, which may be absolute or conditional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following must be fasted on consecutive days (mutatabi')?
A) Make-up (qada) of Ramadan fasts
B) Voluntary (nafl) fasts
C) Kaffara for zihar
D) Fidyat al-halq for harm on the head

Key Takeaway
Missed fasts must be compensated by fidya via bequest; the bequest is wajib and executed from the one-third estate after debts; fasts requiring consecutive days are specified by text and hadith.

Fidyah for Permanent Incapacity: Original vs. Substitute Fasts
Ruling on fidyah for permanent incapacity to fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which fidyah is wajib in the Hanafi school and apply the rule that fidyah is only valid for original obligations, not for substitute fasts.

Core Idea
Fidyah is wajib for permanent incapacity, but only for original fasts, not for expiation substitutes.

In this passage, the Hanafi school clarifies a critical distinction about fidyah. While the elderly and permanently incapacitated must pay fidyah for missed Ramadan fasts, this compensation is not allowed for other fasts that are substitutes for monetary expiation. Understanding this difference prevents a common mistake.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (fidyah for the elderly) and what level of obligation it carries (wajib).
Fidyah is not fard because it compensates a missed act, not the act itself.
2
Determine the conditions for fidyah
List the conditions mentioned in the text: permanent incapacity until death, the fast being an original obligation (asl).
Also note that travelers who die before residency are exempt.
3
Apply the asl vs badal distinction
Distinguish between original obligations (Ramadan, vowed perpetual fasts) and substitutes (expiation fasts). The text explicitly restricts fidyah to asl.
This is the critical refinement of the passage.
4
Understand the method of giving fidyah
Note that fidyah can be given either by ibaha (inviting to eat) or tamlik (ownership transfer), unlike fitr zakat which requires tamlik.
The passage ends with a general rule about items legislated with 'it'am' vs 'ita.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
سمي كتاب الصوم الأداء نصف القضاء لا يقع إلا عن الأداء كما تقدم (ولا) فدية بالتأخير إليه لإطلاق النص (ويجوز الفطر لشيخ فان وعجوز فانية) فانياً لأنه قرب إلى الفناء، أو فنيت قوته وعجز عن (وتلزمهما الفدية) وكذا من عجز عن نذراً لا بدلاً لغيرهم من ذوي الأعذار (لكل يوم صاع من بر) أو قيمته بشرط دوام عجز الفاني والفانية إلى الموت، ولو كان مسافراً، أو مات قبل الإقامة لا تجب عليه الفدية بفطره في السفر كمن) نذر صوم الأبد فضعف عنه) لاشتغاله بالمعيشة يفطر ويفدي للتيقن بعدم قدرته على القضاء فإن لم يقدر) من تجوز له الفدية (على الفدية لعسرته يستغفر الله سبحانه ويستقيله) أي يطلب منه العفو عن تقصيره في حقه (و) لا تجوز الفدية إلا عن صوم هو أصل بنفسه لا بدل عن غيره حتى لو وجبت عليه كفارة يمين أو قتل أو ظهار أو إفطار فلم يجد ما يكفر به من عتق) وإطعام وكسوة (وهو شيخ فان أو لم يصم) حال قدرته على الصوم حتى صار فانياً (لا تجوز له الفدية) لأن الصوم هنا بدل عن غير وهو التكفير بالمال ولذا لا يجوز المصير إلى الصوم إلا عند العجز عما يكفر به من المال فإن أوصى بالتكفير نفذ من الثلث ويجوز في الفدية الإباحة في الطعام أكلتان مشبعتان لليوم كما يجوز التمليك بخلاف صدقة الفطر فإنه لا بد فيها من التمليك كالزكاة اعلم أن ما شرع بلفظ الإطعام أو الطعام يجوز فيه التمليك والإباحة، وما شرع بلفظ الايتاء أو الأداء يشترط فيه التمليك
Book of Fasting. It is called the Book of Fasting. The performance does not fall except for the performance as preceded. And there is no fidyah for delay due to the unrestricted text. And it is permissible to break the fast for a decaying old man and a decaying old woman, due to approaching decay, or their strength has waned and they are incapable. And fidyah is obligatory upon them, and likewise for one who is incapable of a vow, not as a substitute for others with excuses. For each day a sa' of wheat or its value, conditioned on the permanence of the incapacity of the decaying ones until death. And if he was a traveler, or died before residence, fidyah is not obligatory upon him for breaking his fast during travel, like one who vowed perpetual fasting and became weak, due to preoccupation with livelihood, he breaks and pays fidyah out of certainty of his inability to make up. Then if he cannot afford the fidyah due to hardship, he asks forgiveness of Allah and seeks His absolution. And fidyah is not permissible except for a fast that is original in itself, not a substitute for something else. So if it becomes obligatory upon him the expiation of an oath or manslaughter or zihar or breaking fast, and he does not find what to expiate with from freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing, and he is a decaying old man or did not fast while able until he became decaying, fidyah is not permissible for him, because fasting here is a substitute for something else, namely monetary expiation. Therefore it is not permissible to resort to fasting except when unable to the monetary expiation. If he bequeaths the expiation, it is executed from one-third. And in fidyah, permissiveness in food is allowed: two satisfying meals per day, as is also ownership transfer, unlike sadaqat al-fitr which necessarily requires ownership transfer like zakat. Know that what is legislated with the word 'it'am' or 'ta'am' permits both ownership transfer and permissiveness, and what is legislated with the word 'ita'' or 'ada' requires ownership transfer.
Source pages p. 688

Commentary
This passage states rulings on fidyah for those permanently unable to fast. The matn first clarifies that qada' (make-up) cannot be performed for a different purpose. Then it permits breaking fast for the elderly (shaykh fan and ajuz faniya) who are decaying towards death or have lost strength. They must pay fidyah (compensation) of a sa' of wheat per day, conditioned on continuous incapacity until death. Travelers who die before residency are exempt. One who vowed perpetual fasting but becomes weak due to livelihood may break and pay fidyah since he cannot make up. If he cannot afford fidyah, he should seek forgiveness. Critically, fidyah is only valid for a fast that is an original obligation (asl), not a substitute (badal) for another act, like expiation for oath, manslaughter, etc. In those cases, even if the person becomes senile, fidyah is not allowed because the fast itself is already a substitute for monetary expiation. The hashya then discusses the method of giving fidyah: both ibaha (giving food to eat) and tamlik (ownership transfer) are allowed, unlike fitr zakat which requires tamlik.

Key Terms
شيخ فان (shaykh fān): An old man whose strength is decaying to the point of near death, unable to fast.
فدية (fidyah): Compensation for missed obligatory fast due to permanent incapacity; a sa' of wheat per day.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership of the food to the recipient, required in fitr zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, fidyah is not permissible for which type of fast?
A) Ramadan fast for elderly
B) Vowed fast for perpetual fasting
C) Expiation fast for an oath
D) Fast for a traveler who dies

Key Takeaway
Fidyah is only valid for original obligations, not for substitute fasts, a critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Breaking a Voluntary Fast
Intra-Hanafi dispute with dalil naqli critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi narrations on breaking a voluntary fast without an excuse and explain which one is considered more preponderant by later scholars.

Core Idea
A volunteer faster may break without excuse according to one Hanafi narration, but the cautious position requires an excuse.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of a volunteer faster (mutatawwi') breaking his fast without a valid excuse. You will encounter two narrations: one from Abu Yusuf permitting it, and the majority 'apparent narration' requiring an excuse. The dispute hinges on the interpretation of Prophetic actions and hadith. By the end, you will understand the proofs and the tarjih (preponderance) given by later Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
What is the exact legal question being disputed? It is: May a person who has voluntarily begun a nafl fast break that fast without a legitimate excuse (such as illness or travel)?
This is not about obligatory fasts, which cannot be broken without excuse.
2
State the Two Hanafi Positions
Name the two narrations: (1) The narration from Abu Yusuf permits breaking without excuse, but requires making up the day (qada'). (2) The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), held by al-Karkhi and Abu Bakr al-Razi, forbids breaking without excuse.
Note the authority of each position: Abu Yusuf is a major mujtahid of the school, but the majority of the early Hanafis followed the stricter view.
3
Examine the Proof for Each Position
For the permissive view: the hadith of Aisha where the Prophet began a voluntary fast but ate when offered food, and then made up the day (as per Nasa'i's addition). For the restrictive view: the hadith 'When invited, if fasting let him pray' (implying he should not eat). Also the principle that initiating a voluntary act creates a commitment (iltizam).
The addition in Nasa'i is authenticated by Abu Muhammad Abd al-Haqq, strengthening the permissive proof.
4
Determine the Tarjih
Al-Kamal (Ibn al-Humam) and al-Tahtawi lean toward the permissive narration as more preponderant (awjah), but note that the fatwa is not definitively settled. In practice, a Hanafi may follow either, though the cautious approach is to have an excuse.
Tarjih here is based on the strength of the hadith evidence (the explicit action of the Prophet) over the general principle of commitment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز للمتطوع بالصوم (الفطر بلا عذر في (رواية) عن أبي يوسف قال الكمال واعتقادي أنها أوجه لما روى مسلم عن عائشة الله رضي عنها أنها قالت: دخل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ذات يوم فقال : هل عندكم شيء فقلنا : لا فقال : إني إذن صائم ثم أتى في يوم آخر فقلنا يا رسول أهدي إلينا حيس فقال : أرنيه فلقد أصبحت صائماً فأكل وزاد النسائي ولكن أصوم يوماً مكانه وصحح هذه الزيادة أبو محمد عبد الحق وذكر الكرخي وأبو بكر أنه ليس له أن يفطر إلا من عذر وهو ظاهر الرواية لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال : إذا دعي أحدكم إلى طعام فليجب فإن كان مفطراً فليأكل وإن كان صائماً فليصل
And it is permissible for the volunteer faster to break his fast without an excuse in a narration from Abu Yusuf. Al-Kamal said: And my belief is that it is more preponderant due to what Muslim narrated from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, that she said: The Prophet entered one day and asked, 'Do you have anything?' We said, 'No.' He said, 'Then I am fasting.' Then another day he came and we said, 'O Messenger of Allah, we have been given some hais (a dish).' He said, 'Show it to me, I began the day fasting,' so he ate. And al-Nasa'i added: 'But I will fast another day in its place.' This addition was authenticated by Abu Muhammad Abd al-Haqq. Al-Karkhi and Abu Bakr mentioned that it is not permissible for him to break except with an excuse, and that is the apparent narration, due to what was narrated that he said: 'When one of you is invited to food, let him respond; if he is not fasting let him eat, and if he is fasting let him pray.'
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a volunteer faster (mutatawwi') breaking his fast without an excuse. The matn states it is permissible in a narration from Abu Yusuf. Al-Kamal (Ibn al-Humam) considers this narration more preponderant (awjah), citing the hadith from Aisha where the Prophet started a voluntary fast but broke it when offered food, and the addition that he made up the day. Al-Tahtawi notes that Abu Muhammad Abd al-Haqq authenticated this addition. However, al-Karkhi and Abu Bakr al-Razi hold the position that breaking without excuse is not permissible, and they consider the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) to be that breaking is only allowed with an excuse. They cite the hadith 'When invited... if fasting let him pray (instead of eating).' The dispute centers on whether the Prophetic action indicates permissibility or requires an excuse. The hashya does not resolve which is mufta bihi, but implies the narration from Abu Yusuf is considered by some as stronger, while the majority (al-Karkhi, Abu Bakr) adhere to the stricter view.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): One who volunteers an act of worship, here specifically voluntary fasting (nafl).
رواية (riwayah): A transmitted narration from an Imam, here referring to the narration from Abu Yusuf.
حيس (hays): A dish made of dates, dried curds, and clarified butter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which hadith addition is considered crucial for the permissive narration, and who authenticated it?
A) The addition 'But I will fast another day' authenticated by al-Nasa'i
B) The addition 'Let him pray' authenticated by al-Bukhari
C) The addition 'When invited, respond' authenticated by Muslim
D) The addition 'I am fasting' authenticated by al-Karkhi

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school contains a valid narration permitting a volunteer faster to break his fast without an excuse, but he must make up the day, and later scholars consider this view more preponderant.

Ruling on Fidyah for Kaffara and Method of Giving
Explains when fidyah is not permissible for a person owing kaffara and the distinction between ibaha and tamlik in fidyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which fidyah is not permissible for a person owing kaffara and distinguish between the permissible methods of giving fidyah (ibaha vs tamlik) with their respective textual basis.

Core Idea
Fidyah cannot substitute for kaffara when fasting is still possible; but in fidyah for fasting, both feeding and ownership transfer are valid.

In this passage, al-Tahtawi clarifies a critical nuance in the Hanafi law of expiations: when a person owes a kaffara but cannot afford the monetary forms, fidyah is not a permissible substitute if they still have the capacity to fast. The passage then shifts to the method of giving fidyah for those who cannot fast in Ramadan, explaining the Hanafi ruling that fidyah can be given as an invitation to eat (ibaha) or as outright ownership (tamlik).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the first part of the passage. A person owes a kaffara (for oath, killing, zihar, or breaking fast) but cannot afford the monetary expiation (freeing slave, feeding, clothing). He is either an old man who has become weak (shaykh fan) or he did not fast while capable until he became weak (fani).
2
Determine the ruling
Notice the ruling: 'فدية لا تجوز' (fidyah is not permissible). Ask: Why? Because fasting here is a substitute for monetary expiation, and one cannot resort to the substitute unless unable to do the original.
3
Contrast with regular fidyah
The passage then moves to fidyah for those unable to fast in Ramadan. Here fidyah is permissible. Note that the text then discusses the method of giving: ibaha (permission to eat) or tamlik (ownership).
4
Distinguish ibaha vs tamlik
Ibaha means inviting the poor and giving them a meal to eat on the spot without transferring ownership. Tamlik means giving the food as a gift so they become owners. Both are valid for this fidyah.
5
Apply the general principle
The commentator gives a rule: acts instituted with the words 'it'am' or 'ta'am' (e.g., kaffara of zihar or breaking fast) allow both methods. Acts with 'ita'' or 'ada'' (like zakat and sadaqat al-fitr) require tamlik. See how this principle is derived from the Qur'an and Hadith.
Memorize this principle: it helps distinguish similar rulings.

Expiation of an oath, or killing, or zihar, or breaking fast, and he did not find what to expiate with [from freeing a slave], and feeding and clothing, and he is an old man who has become weak, or he did not fast while capable until he became weak, then fidyah is not permissible for him. Because fasting here is a substitute for something else, which is monetary expiation. Therefore, one does not resort to fasting except when unable to do monetary expiation. If he bequeaths the expiation, it is executed from one-third. In fidyah, it is permissible to give food as permission (ibaha) - two full meals for a day - as it is permissible to give as ownership (tamlik). Unlike sadaqat al-fitr, which must be given as ownership like zakat. Know that what is instituted with the word 'it'am' or 'ta'am' allows both tamlik and ibaha, and what is instituted with the word 'ita'' or 'ada'' requires tamlik. His statement: '(or killing)' means killing a person by mistake. His statement: '(from freeing a slave)' is general for the four expiations, and his statement 'and feeding and clothing' is specific to expiation of an oath. As for killing, there is no feeding in it, nor clothing. As for zihar, there is feeding in it but after fasting, and similarly for breaking fast. His statement: '(or he did not fast)' is in contrast to his statement 'he is an old man who has become weak', meaning that there is no difference in the impermissibility of fidyah in fasting which is not original between... [OCR unclear] ...and he is capable, then he delayed until he became weak, and between when its cause arises from zihar or... in his state of weakness. His statement: '(and therefore it is not permissible)' meaning because fasting here is a substitute. His statement: '(two full meals)' with fatha on the hamza, dual of 'akla' meaning one eating, not with damm because that means a morsel. His statement: '(for a day)' meaning for fidyah of each day. His statement: 'with the word it'am' like expiation of the mazahir (one who does zihar) and the one who breaks fast in Ramadan. His statement: '(or food)' and it is the compensation for game killed in the sanctuary or ihram, for Allah said: 'and its expiation is feeding of the poor.' His statement: '(with the word ita')' like zakat, for Allah said: 'and give the zakat.' His statement: '(or ada')' as in zakat al-fitr, for it has been narrated: 'give on behalf of every free and slave, young and old, half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of barley.'
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Maraqi al-Falah, with commentary from al-Tahtawi. The ruling addresses a specific scenario: a person owes an expiation (kaffara) for an oath, accidental killing, zihar, or breaking fast in Ramadan. If he cannot afford the monetary expiation (freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing) and he is an old man who has become physically incapable (shaykh fan), or he did not fast while he was capable until he became weak (fani), then fidyah is not permissible for him. The reason is that in this case, fasting is a substitute for the monetary expiation, and one can only resort to the substitute when unable to perform the original. If he bequeaths the expiation (i.e., instructs in his will that it be done after his death), it is executed from one-third of his estate. Then the text moves to fidyah for fasting (the fidyah paid by those unable to fast in Ramadan). It states that in fidyah, it is permissible to give food in two ways: either as ibaha (giving permission to eat, e.g., inviting to a meal) or as tamlik (transferring ownership of the food to the recipient). This contrasts with sadaqat al-fitr (zakat al-fitr) which must be given as tamlik, like zakat. The commentator then provides a general principle: acts instituted with the words 'it'am' or 'ta'am' (e.g., kaffarat al-muzahir, kaffarat al-fitr) allow both tamlik and ibaha. Acts instituted with 'ita'' or 'ada'' (e.g., zakat, sadaqat al-fitr) require tamlik. The commentary then explains each phrase in the matn: 'or killing' means accidental killing; 'from freeing a slave' is general for the four expiations, but feeding and clothing are specific to oath expiation; for killing and zihar, the expiation includes freeing slaves and feeding, but the order differs. The commentator clarifies that the impermissibility of fidyah for the one who delayed fasting until death applies whether he was capable at first or not, because the fast here is not the original obligation but a substitute.

Key Terms
فدية (fidyah): Compensation paid by a person unable to fast, typically by feeding the poor.
إباحة (ibaha): Giving food as a meal where the recipient is allowed to eat without owning the food.
تمليك (tamlik): Transferring ownership of the food to the recipient.
كفارة (kaffara): Expiation for a sin or violation, such as breaking an oath, accidental killing, zihar, or breaking fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is permissible if the person bequeaths it
C) It is not permissible if the person is capable of fasting
D) It is always permissible as an alternative

Key Takeaway
Fidyah is not a substitute for kaffara when the person can still fast; in fidyah for fasting, both ibaha and tamlik are valid, unlike sadaqat al-fitr which requires tamlik.

Voluntary Faster Breaking Fast Without Excuse
Intra-Hanafi dispute on breaking voluntary fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on a voluntary faster breaking his fast without excuse, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Breaking a voluntary fast without excuse is makruh tahrimi, not haram, but requires qada'.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute over a seemingly simple act: can a person who voluntarily starts a supererogatory fast break it without a valid reason? The passage presents two narrations within the Hanafi school, supported by hadith evidence, and explains how later scholars reconciled them into a single relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Hanafi positions
The apparent riwaya (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that breaking a voluntary fast without excuse is not permissible. Abu Yusuf's narration holds it is permissible. Identify each position clearly.
The text presents Abu Yusuf's view first, then the apparent riwaya.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's proof
Study the hadith of Aisha (from Muslim and Nasa'i) where the Prophet broke his voluntary fast after starting it, and the addition 'but I will fast a day in its place'.
This hadith is the main evidence for permissibility.
3
Examine the apparent riwaya's proof
Consider the hadith about invitation: 'When invited, if fasting, let him pray (supplicate).' Al-Qurtubi authenticated this and argued that breaking would be better if permissible.
4
Reconcile the two proofs
The passage reconciles by noting that the Prophet's breaking might have been due to an excuse (the gift of hays). The addition of qada' shows that it is not a purely voluntary act without consequences.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its implication
Later scholars (e.g., al-Kamal) preferred the view of permissibility but the relied-upon ruling is that it is makruh tahrimi and requires qada'. Understand that this is not haram because the evidence is not definitive.
The practical consequence: if broken, one must make up the fast but does not commit a major sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ويجوز للمتطوع) بالصوم (الفطر بلا عذر في (رواية) عن أبي يوسف قال الكمال واعتقادي أنها أوجه لما روى مسلم عن عائشة الله رضي | عنها أنها قالت: دخل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ذات يوم فقال : هل عندكم شيء فقلنا : لا فقال : إني إذن صائم ثم أتى في يوم آخر فقلنا يا رسول أهدي إلينا حيس فقال : أرنيه فلقد أصبحت صائماً فأكل وزاد النسائي ولكن أصوم يوماً مكانه وصحح هذه الزيادة أبو محمد عبد الحق وذكر الكرخي وأبو بكر أنه ليس له أن يفطر إلا من عذر وهو ظاهر الرواية لما . روي الصلاة والسلام قال : إذا م دعي أحدكم إلى طعام فليجب فإن كان مفطراً فليأكل وإن كان صائماً فليصل قوله: (فليصل) أي فليدع قال القرطبي : ثبت هذا الحديث عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام ولو كان الفطر جائزاً كان الأفضل الفطر لإجابة الدعوة التي السنة، وصححه في المحيط اعلم أن إفساد الصوم بلا عذر بعد الشروع فيهما نقلاً مكروه وليس بحرام لأن الدليل ليس قطعي الدلالة، وإن لزم القضاء، وإذا عرض عذر أبيح للمتطوع الفطر اتفاقاً قوله: (فقال إني إذن صائم صريح في صح النية نهاراً في النفل كما هو المذهب. قوله: (أهدى إلينا حيس هو تمر ينزع نواه، ويدق مع الإقط، ويعجنان بالسمن، ثم يدلك باليد حتى يبقى كالثريد، وهو في الأصل مصدر يقال: حاس الرجل حيساً إذا اتخذ ذلك قاله السيد في الحاشية عن المصباح والإقط مثلثة ،وتحرك وككتف ورجل وابل شيء يتخذ من المخيض الغنمي والمخيض هو اللبن الذي أخذ ،زبده والمضارع مثلث الخاء قاموس .
(And it is permissible for the voluntary faster) to break his fast without excuse in a narration from Abu Yusuf. Al-Kamal said: I believe it is more likely because of what Muslim narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she said: The Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) entered one day and said, 'Do you have anything?' We said, 'No.' He said, 'Then I am fasting.' Then he came another day and we said, 'O Messenger of Allah, we have been given some hays (a type of food).' He said, 'Show it to me. I started the day fasting.' So he ate. An-Nasa'i added: 'But I will fast a day in its place.' Abu Muhammad Abd al-Haqq authenticated this addition. Al-Karkhi and Abu Bakr mentioned that he should not break his fast except for an excuse, and that is the apparent riwaya. For it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) said: 'When one of you is invited to food, let him accept. If he is not fasting, let him eat; if he is fasting, let him pray (i.e., supplicate).' His statement: '(let him pray)' means let him supplicate. Al-Qurtubi said: This hadith is firmly established from him (peace be upon him). Even if breaking the fast were permissible, the better would be to break it to respond to the invitation, which is sunnah. He authenticated it in al-Muhit. Know that spoiling the fast without an excuse after beginning it is, according to reliable transmission, makruh and not haram, because the evidence is not definitive in meaning, and qada' is required. If an excuse arises, it becomes permissible for the voluntary faster to break his fast by agreement. His statement: '(He said: Then I am fasting)' is explicit in the validity of intention during the day for a supererogatory fast, as is the madhhab. His statement: '(hays was given to us)' it is dates whose pits are removed, crushed with dried yogurt, mixed with samn (clarified butter), then rubbed by hand until it becomes like tharid. It is originally a verbal noun. It is said: 'has ar-rajulu haysan' if he does that, as mentioned by as-Sayyid in the hashya from al-Misbah. Al-iqt (dried yogurt) has three possible vowelizations, etc.
Source pages p. 689

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a voluntary faster may break his fast without a valid excuse. The matn (from Nur al-Idah or Maraqi al-Falah) notes a narration from Abu Yusuf that it is permissible. Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam considers this view more likely, citing a hadith from Aisha where the Prophet broke his voluntary fast after starting it. The hadith includes an addition by al-Nasa'i that the Prophet said he would fast another day in its place, which supports permissibility. However, the apparent riwaya (zahir al-riwaya) from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is that it is not permissible except for an excuse. The commentary mentions that al-Karkhi and Abu Bakr al-Jassas held this view. The text then discusses a second hadith: 'When invited, if fasting, let him pray (supplicate).' Al-Qurtubi interprets this as a recommendation to break the fast for the invitation if permissible. The commentator concludes that the ruling is that breaking a voluntary fast without excuse is makruh (specifically makruh tahrimi) and not haram, because the Quranic evidence ('Do not invalidate your deeds') is not definitive in meaning. However, qada' is required if broken. If an excuse arises, it is unanimously permitted. The commentary then explains the hadith phrases: the Prophet's statement 'then I am fasting' shows the validity of making intention for a supererogatory fast during the day, which is the Hanafi position. The term 'hays' is defined as a food made from dates, dried yogurt, and clarified butter.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): One who performs a supererogatory act of worship voluntarily.
حيس (hays): A type of food made from dates, dried yogurt, and clarified butter.
كراهة تحريم (karahah tahrimi): Prohibitively disliked; a category between haram and makruh tanzih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram and requires qada' and kaffara
B) It is makruh tahrimi and requires qada'
C) It is permissible without any compensation
D) It is makruh tanzih without qada'

Key Takeaway
A voluntary faster who breaks his fast without excuse must make up the day, though the act is not sinful enough to be haram; it is makruh tahrimi.

Hospitality as an Excuse to Break a Voluntary Fast: Conditions and Exceptions
States the ruling that hospitality is a valid excuse for a guest and host to break a voluntary fast before zawal, with exceptions for parents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hospitality as an excuse to break a voluntary fast, identify the conditions and exceptions, and explain the evidence.

Core Idea
Hospitality is a valid excuse to break a voluntary fast before zawal, but not after, except to avoid disobeying parents.

Hospitality (diyafah) plays a significant role in Muslim social life, and the Hanafi school acknowledges it as a valid reason to break a voluntary fast under certain conditions. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof from the Sunnah, and the nuances regarding timing and parental rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Scope
The passage states that hospitality is an excuse for both the guest and the host to break a voluntary fast, but only according to the most apparent view (al-azhar). Note that this applies only to voluntary (nafl) fasts, not obligatory ones (Ramadan, qada, or nadhr).
The ruling is permissive, not obligatory; breaking the fast is allowed, not required.
2
Understand the Time Restriction
The excuse is valid only before zawal (midday). After zawal, the fast is considered more established (mutakakkid), and breaking it is not permitted, unless a stronger reason overrides.
The wisdom behind this distinction is that the body experiences more difficulty later in the day, making the fast more confirmed.
3
Analyze the Exception for Parents
After zawal, if not breaking the fast would lead to disobedience (uqoq) toward one's parents, then breaking is allowed until asr (late afternoon). This is because the right of parents is emphasized and avoiding uqoq takes precedence.
This exception applies only to parents, not to any other relatives or individuals.
4
Examine the Intra-Madhhab Dispute
There are multiple opinions within the Hanafi school: some say hospitality is never an excuse, some say it is always an excuse, and some condition it on the host being offended and the guest trusting himself to make up the fast. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) combines these conditions.
The most sound position according to later authorities (ad-Durr, at-Tanwir) is that the host must be displeased with mere presence and the guest should be able to make up the fast later.
5
Recognize the Evidence (Dalil)
The proof is a hadith narrated by Tabarani from Ibn Umar (ra): 'When one of you enters upon his Muslim brother, if he wants to break his fast, let him break it, unless his fast is Ramadan, qada of Ramadan, or a vow.' This indicates permissibility for voluntary fasts.
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator), thus zanni in transmission and meaning, but sufficient for a ruling on voluntary acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(والضيافة عذر على الأظهر للضيف والمضيف) فيما قبل الزوال لا بعده إلا أن يكون في عدم فطره بعده عقوق لأحد الأبوين لا غيرهما للتأكد قوله: (والضيافة عذر على الأظهر لما رواه الطبراني في كبيره عن ابن عمر قال : إذا دخل أحدكم على أخيه المسلم فإن أراد أن يفطر فليفطر إلا أن يكون صومه ذلك رمضان، أو قضاء رمضان، أو نذراً ا هـ كذا في الجامع الصغير للسيوطي . قوله : (على الأظهر) وقيل : عذر وقيل : ليست بعذر مطلقاً، وقيل عذران وثق من نفسه بالقضاء، وإن كان لا يثق لا يفطر وإن كان في ترك الإفطار أذى أخيه المسلم قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني : وهو أحسن ما قيل في هذا الباب ،بحر، وقيد صاحب التنوير العذر بها بما إذا كان صاحبها ممن لا يرضى بمجرد حضوره، ويتأذى بترك الإفطار وإلا فلا. قال في الدر عن الظهيرية: وهو الصحيح من المذهب . قوله : (للضيف يقال للواحد والجمع، ويجمع على أضياف، وضيوف وضيفان . قوله: (والمضيف) بفتح الميم أصله مضيوف، وفي عبارة القاموس ما يفيد أنه يقال مضاف . قوله : (إلا أن يكون في عدم قطره بعده عقوق لأحد الأبوين) فيفطر بعده إلى العصر لا بعده في الدر. قوله: (للتأكد) أي تأكد حق الوالدين وفي الشرح ما يفيد أنه علة لقوله، لا بعده، وعبارته ووجه الفرق أن الصوم في أول اليوم لا يتأكد عادة لما عرف أنه لا يشتد على البدن، ولا كذلك بعد الزوال ا هـ بتصرف فإن قوله : ولا كذلك بعد الزوال أي فإنه يتأكد ا هـ .
(And hospitality is an excuse according to the most apparent view, for the guest and the host) before zawal (zenith), not after it, unless not breaking it after that would be disobedience to one of the parents, no one else, due to emphasis (of their right). His statement: '(and hospitality is an excuse according to the most apparent view)' because of what at-Tabarani narrated in his Mu'jam al-Kabir from Ibn Umar who said: 'When one of you enters upon his Muslim brother, if he wants to break his fast, let him break it, unless his fast is Ramadan, or qada' of Ramadan, or a vow.' This is from al-Jami' as-Saghir of as-Suyuti. His statement: '(according to the most apparent view)' and it has been said: [it is] an excuse, and it has been said: not an excuse at all, and it has been said: it is an excuse if one trusts himself to make up the fast, but if he does not trust, he should not break it even if abandoning it harms his Muslim brother. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: This is the best that has been said on this subject (al-Bahr). The author of at-Tanwir restricted the excuse to when the host is not pleased with mere presence and is hurt by not breaking the fast, otherwise no. He said in ad-Durr from az-Zahiriyya: This is the correct position in the madhhab. His statement: '(for the guest)' – it is used for singular and plural, and is pluralized as adyaf, duyuf, and dayfan. His statement: '(and the host)' with fatha on the mim, originally madyuf, and from the Qamus it is said mudaf. His statement: '(unless not breaking it after that would be disobedience to one of the parents)' – then he breaks it after that until asr, not after that, in ad-Durr. His statement: '(due to emphasis)' i.e., the emphasis of the rights of parents; in the sharh it is indicated that it is the reason for his statement 'not after it', and his words: 'and the difference is that fasting in the first part of the day is usually not confirmed (i.e., it is easier) because it is known that it does not become intense on the body, unlike after zawal' – he said: 'after zawal' it becomes intense.
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that hospitality (diyafah) is a valid excuse for a guest or host to break a voluntary fast, according to the most apparent view (al-azhar) in the Hanafi madhhab. This applies before midday (zawal), not after, unless failing to break fast after zawal would cause disobedience to parents (uqoq), in which case one may break to avoid that. The commentator provides evidence from a hadith narrated by at-Tabarani from Ibn Umar, which permits breaking a voluntary fast when visiting a brother, unless the fast is obligatory (Ramadan, qada, or vow). There are multiple scholarly opinions: some say it is never an excuse, some say it is always an excuse, and some say it depends on the host's attitude. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani considers the condition that one trusts himself to make up the fast to be the best. The author of at-Tanwir adds that the excuse applies only if the host is not content with the guest merely being present and would be offended by him not breaking the fast. The correct position in the madhhab according to ad-Durr from az-Zahiriyya is that hospitality is an excuse. The commentator then explains lexical points: 'dayf' is used for both singular and plural; 'mudif' (host) has different forms. He also clarifies the exception for parents: if not breaking fast after zawal would be disobedience, one may break until asr, but not later (in ad-Durr). The reason for the distinction between before and after zawal is that fasting in the first part of the day is not yet firmly established (i.e., it is easier to break), while after zawal it becomes more serious (the body has started to suffer).

Key Terms
ضيافة (diyafah): Hospitality, the act of hosting a guest and providing food.
عقوق (uqoq): Disobedience or disrespect towards parents.
الزوال (zawal): The sun's zenith; midday.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Before zawal only
B) After zawal only
C) At any time
D) Only before zawal, but after zawal only if abandoning it would be disobedience to parents

Key Takeaway
Hospitality excuses breaking a voluntary fast before midday; after midday, only the risk of disobeying parents permits it.

Oath to Break a Fast: Relied-Upon Ruling
Gives relied-upon ruling for oath to break fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling for a person who swears by divorce to break his fast, identify the intra-madhhab dispute regarding timing, and explain the legal reasoning (ri'ayat haqq al-akh) and the resolution of an apparent contradiction (istishkal).

Core Idea
A person who swears by divorce to break his fast must actually break it, even after midday, to protect his brother's right.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a specific scenario: a person makes an oath using the formula of divorce that he will break his fast. The relied-upon ruling in the Hanafi school is clear, yet there is a subtle intra-madhhab difference and an apparent contradiction that requires reconciliation. In this lesson, you will learn the mufta bihi position and the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the oath
Note the exact scenario: a person swears by divorce that he will break his fast. The oath is binding and requires action.
The oath uses 'talaq' (divorce) as a formula, which is a strong oath.
2
State the relied-upon ruling
According to the text, al-i'timad is that he breaks his fast even after zawal, and he is not considered hanith (sinful for breaking the oath).
The ruling is from at-Tanwir and its commentary.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some scholars (like the author of an-Nahr) restrict the ruling to before zawal. The relied-upon view extends it to after zawal. The reason given is ri'ayat haqq al-akh.
This shows the importance of social considerations in fiqh.
4
Analyze the apparent contradiction (istishkal)
There is an apparent contradiction: normally, a person who swears to do something he does not own (like breaking an act of worship) would be absolved by mere speech. The reconciliation is that here the oath is conditional or that not breaking would incur sin.
The istishkal is resolved by the Hanafi response.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa is that he must break his fast even after zawal, and he is not sinful. This is based on fatawa mutakhkhirin.
This ruling prioritizes brotherly relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلف شخص بالطلاق ليفطرن فالاعتماد على أنه يفطر ولو بعد الزوال ولا يحنثه لرعاية حق أخيه وله البشارة بهذه الفائدة الجليلة) قال في قوله : (بالطلاق) أطلقه فعم الرجعي وهل العتق مثله يحرر . قوله : فالاعتماد على أنه يفطر) ولو كان صائماً قضاء تنوير وشرحه. قوله: (ولو) بعد الزوال) الذي يلوح من عبارة صاحب النهر أن ذلك فيما إذا كان قبل الزوال لا بعده قوله : (ولا يحنثه) استشكل بما هو مصرح به من في الحلف على ما لا يملك يبر بمجرد القول فيبر بقوله أفطر، ويمكن التوفيق بحمل ما هنا مما يقتضي أنه إن لم يفطر يحنث على ما إذا كان الحلف بطريق التعليق، أو يحمل على ما إذا لم يأمره بالفعل قاله السيد في حاشية الأشباه : قوله : (الرعاية حق أخيه علة لقوله : يفطر . قوله :
And if a person swears by divorce that he will definitely break his fast, then the reliance is that he breaks his fast, even after zawal, and he is not considered disgraced (or the oath is not broken?) out of consideration for the right of his brother, and he may be glad about this noble benefit. He said: His statement: '(by divorce)' – he made it general, so it includes revocable divorce. And is slavery like it? It needs to be investigated. His statement: 'the reliance is that he breaks his fast' – even if he is fasting qada'; [this is from] at-Tanwir and its commentary. His statement: '(even after zawal)' – what appears from the statement of the author of an-Nahr is that this is when it is before zawal, not after. His statement: '(and he is not disgraced)' – it is problematic because what is explicitly stated is that one who swears about something he does not own is absolved by mere speech, so he is absolved by saying 'I break fast'. And reconciliation is possible by taking what is here as implying that if he does not break, he is sinful, based on the oath being conditional, or by taking it as if he does not command the action. This is said by as-Sayyid in Hashiyat al-Ashbah. His statement: '(consideration of the right of his brother)' is the reason for his statement 'he breaks his fast'. His statement: ...
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case: a person swears an oath using the formula of divorce (i.e., he says 'My wife is divorced if I do not break my fast') that he will break his fast. The relied-upon ruling (al-i'timad) is that he must break his fast, even if it is after zawal (midday). He will not be considered to have committed a sin or broken the oath, out of consideration for his brother's rights (i.e., the host's rights). The commentator explains that the word 'talaq' here is general, including revocable divorce. He then presents a discussion: some scholars (like the author of an-Nahr) restrict this to before zawal, but the relied-upon view extends it to after zawal. There is an apparent contradiction: ordinarily, if one swears to do something he does not own (like breaking a fast is an act of worship that he may not own the right to break), he would be absolved by merely saying 'I break fast' without actually acting. The reconciliation is that here, if he does not actually break, he would be sinful (in violating the oath) because the oath is conditional or because he is commanded to actually break. The reason for this ruling is the consideration of the brother's right (the host who will be offended). This is a noble benefit (fa'ida jalila).

Key Terms
إعتماد (i'timad): The relied-upon view in the madhhab for giving fatwa.
حنث (hanith): To break an oath, incurring sin or penalty.
رعاية حق الأخ (ri'ayat haqq al-akh): Consideration for the right of the Muslim brother (the host).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must not break his fast, and the divorce takes effect
B) He should break his fast only before zawal, otherwise the oath is broken
C) He should break his fast even after zawal, and he is not considered sinful
D) He should break his fast only if he trusts himself to make it up later

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi ruling is that one must break his fast when sworn by oath of divorce, even after zawal, to preserve the brother's right, and he is not considered a sinner.

Breaking a Voluntary Fast: Makruh Tahrimi Due to Zanni Proof
States the legal ruling on breaking a voluntary fast without excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules breaking a voluntary fast as makruh tahrimi and identify the role of zanni dalala in the ruling.

Core Idea
Breaking a voluntary fast is makruh tahrimi because its proof is zanni, not qat'i.

The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between haram and makruh tahrimi based on the certainty of the proof. In this passage, the author clarifies that breaking a voluntary fast without excuse is not haram but makruh tahrimi, because the Quranic verse cited as proof is open to interpretation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific act being ruled upon? What is the hukm level? Note that the text explicitly says it is makruh (tahrimi) and not haram.
The act is breaking a voluntary fast without excuse after starting it.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite for the ruling? The verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (Surah Muhammad 47:33) is referenced.
The dalil is Quranic, with qat'i thubut but zanni dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof lead to the ruling? The imperative 'do not invalidate' could mean invalidating reward, not the act itself, making the meaning uncertain (zanni). Hence the ruling is below haram.
The uncertainty prevents the ruling from being haram.
4
Understand the consequence: qada required
Even though it is not haram, qada (making up the fast) is still required because the obligation to complete the fast remains.
This shows that makruh tahrimi still carries a compensatory duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن إفساد الصوم بلا عذر بعد الشروع فيهما نقلاً مكروه وليس بحرام لأن الدليل ليس قطعي الدلالة، وإن لزم القضاء [قوله مكروه) الظاهر من إطلاقهم أنها كراهة تحريم. قوله : (لأن الدليل) وهو قوله تعالى : ولا تبطلوا أعمالكم ) . قوله : (ليس قطعي الدلالة) لاحتمال أن يكون المعنى والله تعالى أعلم، ولا تبطلوا ثواب أعمالكم بنحو رياء وسمعة.
Know that corrupting the fast without excuse after starting it is, according to transmission, disliked (makruh) and not forbidden (haram), because the proof is not definitive in its indication, although qada is required. [His saying: 'makruh'] – the apparent from their absolute statements is that it is makruh tahrimi. His saying: 'because the proof' – which is His statement: 'And do not invalidate your deeds.' His saying: 'not definitive in its indication' – because the meaning could be, and Allah knows best, 'Do not invalidate the reward of your deeds through show and reputation.'
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hashiya. It clarifies that breaking a voluntary fast without a valid excuse after starting it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), not haram. The reasoning is that the proof, the Quranic verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (Muhammad 47:33), is zanni in its meaning—it could refer to invalidating reward through riya, not the act itself. Hence the ruling is makruh tahrimi, which is a level below haram, reflecting the Hanafi principle that acts are haram only when the proof is qat'i. Qada (making up the fast) is still required because the obligation to complete the fast remains.

Key Terms
مكروه تحريما (makruh tahrimi): An act that is proven by a zanni proof and is prohibited but not haram; omitting it is rewarded, but doing it is sinless though blameworthy.
قطعي الدلالة (qat'i al-dalala): A proof whose meaning is definitive and unambiguous, yielding certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the act is recommended
B) Because the proof (Quranic verse) is zanni in its indication, not qat'i
C) Because there is no proof at all
D) Because it is always permissible to break a voluntary fast

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the ruling of makruh tahrimi applies to breaking a voluntary fast without excuse because the Quranic proof is zanni al-dalala, yet qada remains obligatory.

Hospitality as an Excuse to Break Voluntary Fast
Explains when hospitality excuses breaking a voluntary fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking a voluntary fast for hospitality, identify the condition regarding the host's offense, and apply it to a scenario.

Core Idea
Hospitality excuses breaking a voluntary fast before noon only if the host would be offended.

Fasting is a commendable act, but the Hanafi school balances it with social harmony. When a guest's fasting causes distress to a host, the law permits breaking the fast to preserve goodwill. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof, and the conditions that make it operative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Breaking a voluntary fast due to hospitality. What is the hukm? Permissible (mubah) under certain conditions.
The passage clarifies it is an excuse (ʿudhr) on the strongest view.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited from al-Tabarani: ‘When one of you enters upon his Muslim brother... let him break it unless it is Ramadan, qada, or a vow.’ This is the proof.
The hadith indicates permission for voluntary fasts.
3
Identify the condition for the excuse
The excuse is only before noon. After noon, it is not permitted unless breaking would avoid disobedience to parents. For the guest, the host must be someone who would be offended by the guest not eating.
This condition comes from al-Tanwir and al-Durr.
4
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
There are multiple views: some say always an excuse, some never, some only if confident of making up. The chosen view is that of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and the author of al-Durr: conditional on the host's offense.
This refinement is the critical contribution of this passage.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that hospitality is an excuse for the guest only if the host would be offended, and only before noon (except for parents after noon).
From al-Durr and al-Zahiriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضيافة عذر على الأظهر للضيف والمضيف) فيما قبل الزوال لا بعده إلا أن يكون في عدم فطره بعده عقوق لأحد الأبوين لا غيرهما للتأكد [صلى الله عليه وسلم] لما رواه الطبراني في كبيره عن ابن عمر قال : إذا دخل أحدكم على أخيه المسلم فإن أراد أن يفطر فليفطر إلا أن يكون صومه ذلك رمضان، أو قضاء رمضان، أو نذراً ا هـ كذا في الجامع الصغير للسيوطي . قوله : (على الأظهر) وقيل : عذر وقيل : ليست بعذر مطلقاً، وقيل عذران وثق من نفسه بالقضاء، وإن كان لا يثق لا يفطر وإن كان في ترك الإفطار أذى أخيه المسلم قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني : وهو أحسن ما قيل في هذا الباب ،بحر، وقيد صاحب التنوير العذر بها بما إذا كان صاحبها ممن لا يرضى بمجرد حضوره، ويتأذى بترك الإفطار وإلا فلا. قال في الدر عن الظهيرية: وهو الصحيح من المذهب .
And hospitality is an excuse according to the strongest view for the guest and the host before noon, not after noon, unless not breaking fast after noon would be disobedience to one of the parents, not others, due to the emphasis [of parental rights] based on what al-Tabarani narrated in his Mu'jam al-Kabir from Ibn 'Umar: 'When one of you enters upon his Muslim brother, if he wants to break his fast, let him break it, unless that fast is Ramadan, or making up Ramadan, or a vow.' End of quote as in al-Jami' al-Saghir of al-Suyuti. His saying: 'on the strongest view' – and it was said: it is an excuse, and it was said: it is not an excuse at all, and it was said: it is an excuse if he trusts himself to make it up, but if he does not trust, he does not break even if leaving breaking causes harm to his Muslim brother. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: 'This is the best that has been said on this topic.' Al-Bahr. And the author of al-Tanwir restricted the excuse by it to [the case] when the host is one who is not satisfied with mere presence and is harmed by not breaking the fast, otherwise not. He said in al-Durr from al-Zahiriyya: 'This is the correct position of the madhhab.'
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about hospitality as an excuse to break a voluntary fast. The strongest view is that both the guest and the host may break their voluntary fast before noon if hospitality demands it; after noon, the fast is not to be broken unless it would cause disobedience to parents (due to the high emphasis on parental rights). The proof is a hadith from Ibn 'Umar narrated by al-Tabarani, allowing breaking an optional fast unless it is Ramadan, qada of Ramadan, or a vow. There are multiple views: some say it is always an excuse, some say never, others say only if one is confident to make up the fast. The preferred opinion according to Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and the text of al-Durr is that it is an excuse only if the host would be offended by the guest not eating. This reflects Hanafi consideration of social harmony.

Key Terms
الضيافة (al-diyafah): Hospitality; the act of a host serving food to a guest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Anytime, even after noon
B) Only before noon, and only if the host would be offended by the guest's fasting
C) Never, because hospitality does not excuse breaking a fast
D) Only for obligatory fasts like Ramadan

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, hospitality excuses breaking a voluntary fast before noon, but only if the host would be offended by the guest's fasting.

Swearing by Divorce to Break a Voluntary Fast: The Relied-Upon Ruling
Ruling on a person who swears by divorce to break a fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swearing by divorce to break a voluntary fast and explain the exception based on preserving brotherly relations.

Core Idea
Swearing by divorce to break a fast is binding; you must break the fast to prevent divorce, even after noon.

This lesson examines a specific scenario where a person swears by divorce to break a voluntary fast. While normally one may not break a voluntary fast without reason, here the Hanafi school gives precedence to preserving brotherly harmony and avoiding the serious consequences of a divorce oath. The relied-upon position is that the person should actually break his fast, even if it is after noon, and he is not considered to have broken his oath. A hadith about great reward supports this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the oath
Recognize that the person swears by divorce (a binding oath) to break a voluntary fast. The fast is not obligatory.
The oath is not a simple verbal promise; it uses the word of divorce.
2
State the normal rule regarding oaths by divorce
In Hanafi fiqh, swearing by divorce to perform an act that one does not own (like breaking a religious act) normally requires only verbal acknowledgment to avoid divorce? Actually the text notes a tension: normally mere speech suffices, but here actual action is required.
The reconciliation is explained later.
3
Recognize the exception for the brother's right
The text establishes that for the sake of a brother's right (e.g., a request to break the fast together), the relied-upon position is to physically break the fast (eat/drink). This overrides the normal rule.
The hadith promises great reward for breaking fast for a brother's right.
4
Note the evidence and resolution of contradiction
The hadith 'Whoever breaks his fast for his brother's right...' is cited. The commentator reconciles the apparent contradiction (that speech normally suffices) by explaining that here the oath may be conditional or the person swore for the brother's sake, requiring actual fulfillment.
This shows careful juristic reasoning (usul).
5
Apply the ruling to practice
In a real situation, a person who has sworn by divorce to break a voluntary fast for a brother must actually eat or drink, even after noon, to avoid divorce and to gain reward.
If the fast is obligatory (Ramadan, qada of Ramadan), the ruling differs; the oath would be void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حلف شخص بالطلاق ليفطرن فالاعتماد على أنه يفطر ولو بعد الزوال ولا يحنثه لرعاية حق أخيه وله البشارة بهذه الفائدة الجليلة) قال في التجنيس والمزيد رجل أصبح صائماً متطوعاً فدخل على أخ من إخوانه فسأله أن يفطر لا بأس بأن يفطر لقول النبي : من أفطر لحق أخيه يكتب له ثواب صوم ألف قضى يوماً يكتب له ثواب صوم الفي يوم ونقله أيضاً في التتارخانية والمحيط والمبسوط [قوله : (بالطلاق) أطلقه فعم الرجعي وهل العتق مثله يحرر . قوله : فالاعتماد على أنه يفطر) ولو كان صائماً قضاء تنوير وشرحه. قوله: (ولو) بعد الزوال) الذي يلوح من عبارة صاحب النهر أن ذلك فيما إذا كان قبل الزوال لا بعده قوله : (ولا يحنثه) استشكل بما هو مصرح به من في الحلف على ما لا يملك يبر بمجرد القول فيبر بقوله أفطر، ويمكن التوفيق بحمل ما هنا مما يقتضي أنه إن لم يفطر يحنث على ما إذا كان الحلف بطريق التعليق، أو يحمل على ما إذا لم يأمره بالفعل قاله السيد في حاشية الأشباه : قوله : (الرعاية حق أخيه علة لقوله : يفطر .
And if a person swears by divorce that he will surely break his fast, the reliance is that he breaks it even after noon, and he does not break his oath for the sake of respecting his brother's right, and he has good news of this great benefit. He said in al-Tajnis and al-Mazid: A man who began a voluntary fast entered upon one of his brothers and asked him to break his fast; there is no harm in his breaking it because of the Prophet's saying: 'Whoever breaks his fast for the sake of his brother's right, there is written for him the reward of a thousand days of fasting; if he makes up a day, there is written for him the reward of two thousand days of fasting.' And it is also transmitted in al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Muhit, and al-Mabsut. [His saying: 'by divorce'] – he made it absolute so it includes revocable divorce. Is manumission similar? It needs investigation. His saying: 'the reliance is that he breaks it' – even if he was fasting as qada, according to al-Tanwir and its commentary. His saying: 'even if after noon' – from the expression of the author of al-Nahr it seems that this applies before noon, not after. His saying: 'he does not break his oath' – this is problematic as it is explicit that one who swears to do what he does not own is absolved by mere speech, so he would be absolved by saying 'I break fast'. It is possible to reconcile by interpreting that here, if he does not break, he breaks his oath, if the oath was conditional, or it applies if he did not command the act, as said by al-Sayyid in Hashiyat al-Ashbah. His saying: 'for the sake of respecting his brother's right' is the reason for his saying: 'he breaks it'.
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on a specific oath scenario. If someone swears by divorce that he will break his voluntary fast, the relied-upon position is that he should actually break his fast, even if it is after noon, for the sake of his brother's right. This is despite the general rule that one who swears to do something he does not own (like breaking a fast which is a religious act) is absolved by merely saying 'I break fast'. Here, the fatwa is to actually break it to maintain harmony. The commentator reconciles by noting that the oath may be conditional or that the person swore for the brother's sake. The hadith about reward (thousand days) is cited to encourage this. There is a nuance: the author of al-Nahr restricts the permission to before noon, while the text says 'even after noon'. The commentator resolves this by noting the fatwa is for breaking after noon when the brother's right is involved.

Key Terms
اليمين بالطلاق (al-yamin bi-al-talaq): An oath using the word of divorce, which has binding effect in Hanafi fiqh; breaking it may trigger divorce.
حنث (hanith): Breaking an oath; the state of having violated a sworn oath.
تطوع (tatowwu'): Voluntary act of worship, supererogatory.

The Virtue of Breaking a Voluntary Fast for a Brother
Narrates a hadith on the reward for breaking a voluntary fast for a brother's sake.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on breaking a voluntary fast for a brother's sake and identify the reward mentioned in the transmitted hadith.

Core Idea
Breaking a voluntary fast for a brother yields immense reward.

This lesson examines a hadith cited in Hanafi sources that speaks of a tremendous reward for one who breaks his voluntary fast for the sake of a brother. Though the hadith is weak in transmission, it is used by Hanafi scholars to encourage maintaining brotherly ties and hospitality, and it establishes that breaking a nafl fast for such a purpose is not only permissible but highly rewarded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is a voluntary (nafl) fast that a man has begun. Determine the legal category: it is a recommended act, but not obligatory.
Voluntary fasts are acts of devotion that one may choose to start and, under certain circumstances, break.
2
Recognize the situation and request
The man enters upon a brother (a fellow Muslim) and is asked to break his fast. The request implies a social or hospitality context.
The brother's 'right' (haqq) is mentioned, indicating a duty of brotherhood.
3
State the ruling and its basis
The text says 'la ba'sa bi an yufatir' – there is no harm in breaking. The ruling is mubah (permissible) but with encouragement. The basis is the Prophet's statement.
The ruling applies specifically to voluntary fasts; obligatory fasts may not be broken.
4
Examine the evidence (the hadith)
The hadith promises a reward of one thousand days of fasting for breaking it, and two thousand days if one makes up the missed day later. This reward shows the high virtue of the act.
Although the hadith is considered weak (daif) in transmission, it is cited in Hanafi texts as part of the school's transmitted proofs (riwayat).
5
Note the transmission and context
The hadith is recorded in al-Tajnis, al-Mazid, al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Muhit, and al-Mabsut, showing its acceptance in the Hanafi tradition as a basis for encouraging brotherly kindness.
The Imam's reliance on such narrations reflects the Hanafi principle of taking ahadith as long as they are not contradictory to stronger proofs.

Source Text

This hadith, though weak in transmission, is cited by Hanafi scholars to encourage maintaining brotherly ties. Qada for Broken Voluntary Fast on Prohibited Days: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada for voluntary fasts on prohibited days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding qada for a broken voluntary fast on the five prohibited days, and explain the qiyas reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Breaking a voluntary fast on Eid: Abu Hanifa says no qada; the Sahibayn say yes.

The general rule in the Hanafi school is that breaking a voluntary fast, whether intentionally or by excuse, requires qada (makeup). This applies even if the fast is invalid from the start. However, there is an internal dispute among the founding imams regarding five specific days where fasting is prohibited: the two Eids and the three days of Tashriq. This lesson will unpack the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa versus that of the two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and show how their different approaches to istihsan and qiyas lead to opposite rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule and its basis
Identify that the Hanafi school unanimously holds qada wajib for breaking a voluntary fast, to preserve the validity of what was begun.
This applies regardless of excuse or intention.
2
Identify the exception: the five prohibited days
List the five days: Eid al-Fitr, Eid al-Adha, and the three days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th Dhul-Hijjah). Note that Abu Hanifa exempts these from qada.
The exception is only in the apparent riwaya from Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that beginning a fast on these days is itself a sin (turning away from Allah's hospitality), so the obligation to complete is negated. Hence, no qada.
The key: the prohibition is immediate upon intention.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning and compare
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad analogize to praying nafl at disliked times: beginning is binding like a vow, so qada is required. Abu Hanifa distinguishes by noting that in prayer, sin only occurs after sajdah, whereas in fasting sin occurs at the start.
This is a qiyas khafi with an illa of ilzam (binding effect).
5
Determine the practical impact (thamarah al-khilaf)
If someone fasts on Eid and breaks it, Abu Hanifa says no qada; the Sahibayn say qada wajib. The later mufta bihi often follows the Sahibayn for caution.
The text does not give explicit tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أفطر) المتطوع على أي حال كان ( عليه القضاء) لا خلاف بين أصحابنا في وجوبه صيانة لما مضى عن البطلان إلا إذا شرع متطوعاً) بالصوم (في خمسة أيام يومي العيدين وأيام التشريق، فلا يلزمه قضاؤها بإفسادها في ظاهر الرواية عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لأن صومها مأمور بنقضه، ولم يجز إتمامه لأنه بنفس الشروع ارتكب المنهي عنه للإعراض عن ضيافة الله فأمر بقطعه وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد عليه القضاء يعني وإن وجب الفطر فيما ذكرنا إشارة إلى قضاء نفل الصلاة الذي قطعه بشروعه عند نحو الطلوع كما تقدم والله الموفق بمنه الأعظم للدين الأقوم. [قوله (وإذا أفطر على أي حال كان سواء كان الفطر لعذر أم لا وسواء أفسده قصداً أم لا، وهذا إذا شرع قصداً فلو شرع فيه ظناً أنه عليه تذكر أنه ليس عليه شيء، فأفطر فوراً فلا قضاء عليه أما لو مضي ساعة لزمه القضاء لأنه يمضيها صار كأنه نوى في هذه الساعة أفاده في البحر، والمراد بالساعة القطعة من الزمن، وانظر ما لو تذكر أنه ليس عليه، ونوى قطعه إلا أنه لم يتعاط مفطراً هل يكون شارعاً، ومقتضى قولهم أنه بنية الفطر لا يكون مفطراً أنه لا يعد إفطاراً، أو يكون مشروعاً، وحرره قوله لا خلاف بين أصحابنا إلا في صائمة تطوعاً عرض لها الحيض ففي القضاء خلاف، والأصح الوجوب . قوله (صيانة لما مضى أي من الشروع عن البطلان فإنه لما أعقب القضاء كان غير باطل بخلاف ما إذا لم يعقبه قوله: (وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد عليه لقضاء) لأنّ الشروع ملزم كالنذر كالشروع في الصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة، ووجه الفرق للإمام أن القضاء بالشروع يبتني على وجوب الإتمام وهو منتف لأنه بنفس الشروع يكون مرتكباً للنهي ، فأمر بقطعه بخلاف النذر حيث لم يصر مرتكباً للنهي بمجرد النذر لأنه التزم طاعة الله تعالى، وإنما المعصية بالفعل، وبخلاف الشروع في الصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة حيث لم يصر مرتكباً للنهي بمجرد الشروع، ولهذا لا يحنث به إن حلف لا يصلي ما لم يسجد، والشروع هو الموجب للقضاء دون الصلاة فصار كالنذر ولأنه يمكنه الأداء بذلك الشروع في الصلاة لا على وجه الكراهة بأن يمسك حتى تبيض الشمس زيلعي . قوله: (وفيما ذكرنا) أي من قوله : لأنه بنفس الشروع ارتكب المنهي عنه الخ فإنه لا يقال في الصلاة أنه بنفس الشروع فيها ارتكب المنهى عنه، بل إنما يكون ذلك بالسجود بدليل مسئلة اليمين. قوله : (عند نحو الطلوع هو الاستواء، والغروب والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم.
And if the voluntary faster breaks his fast in any case, qada is obligatory upon him. There is no disagreement among our companions regarding its obligation, to preserve what has passed from nullity, except if he began a voluntary fast on five days: the two Eids and the days of Tashriq. Then qada is not required of him if he spoils it, in the apparent riwaya from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), because its fast is commanded to be broken, and its completion is not permissible because by the very beginning he committed a prohibited act by turning away from Allah's hospitality, so he was commanded to cut it. And from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, qada is required, meaning even though breaking is obligatory in what we mentioned, it is an indication to the qada of the nafl prayer which he cut off by beginning it at times like sunrise, as previously mentioned. And Allah is the grantor of success. [His saying: 'And if he breaks fast in any case'] – whether the breaking is for an excuse or not, whether he spoiled it intentionally or not. This applies if he began intentionally. If he began thinking it was obligatory upon him but then remembered it was not, and broke immediately, there is no qada. But if time passed, qada is required, because by passing time it became as if he intended in that hour. This is from al-Bahr. 'Hour' means a portion of time. Consider if he remembered it was not upon him and intended to cut it but did not consume a nullifier: is he considered as having begun? The implication of their saying that by the intention to break he is not a breaker means it is not counted as breaking, or is it considered a beginning? He should verify. His saying: 'There is no disagreement among our companions except regarding a woman who began a voluntary fast and then menstruation occurred; there is disagreement on qada, and the most correct is obligation.' His saying: 'to preserve what has passed' means to preserve the beginning from nullity, because when it is followed by qada, it is not nullified, unlike if it is not followed by qada. His saying: 'And from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, qada is required' because the beginning is binding like a vow, like beginning prayer in disliked times. The reasoning for the Imam [Abu Hanifa] is that qada due to beginning is based on the obligation to complete, and that is negated because by the very beginning he is a perpetrator of a prohibition, so he was commanded to cut it, unlike a vow where he does not become a perpetrator of a prohibition by merely vowing, because he obliged himself to obey Allah, and sin is only in the action; and unlike beginning prayer in disliked times where he does not become a perpetrator of a prohibition by merely beginning, and therefore he does not break his oath if he swore not to pray as long as he does not prostrate. The beginning is what obligates qada, not the prayer, so it becomes like a vow, and also because he can perform the prayer in that beginning not in a disliked manner by delaying until the sun becomes white. (From Zayla'i) His saying: 'and in what we mentioned' i.e., his saying 'because by the very beginning he committed the prohibited act' etc., for it is not said regarding prayer that by the very beginning of it he committed the prohibited act; rather that is only by prostration, as evidenced by the oath issue. His saying: 'at times like sunrise' means at the moment of passing the zenith [or sunrise?], and sunset. And Allah Almighty knows best, and I seek forgiveness from Allah the Magnificent.
Source pages p. 691

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the obligation of qada (making up) for a broken voluntary fast. The general rule, agreed by all Hanafi imams, is that qada is obligatory whenever one breaks a voluntary fast, whether intentionally or due to an excuse, in order to preserve the validity of the act begun. However, there is a disagreement regarding five specific days: the two Eids (al-Fitr and al-Adha) and the three days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul-Hijjah). According to the apparent riwaya from Imam Abu Hanifa, if one begins a voluntary fast on these days and then breaks it, no qada is required. The reason is that these days are inherently prohibited for fasting; the very act of beginning the fast is a sin because it turns away from Allah's 'hospitality' (the festive days). Thus, the beginner is commanded to break it, and the obligation to complete is absent. Therefore, qada is not needed. Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad disagree. They argue that the beginning itself is binding like a vow (nadhr). Just as one who begins a prayer in a disliked time must complete it and make it up if broken, similarly, beginning a voluntary fast creates an obligation that is not lifted by the prohibition. Their analogy is with nafl prayer at sunrise or sunset: the beginning is makruh but qada is still required if broken. Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes by noting that in prayer, the prohibition is not triggered until the first sajdah, whereas in fasting, the very intention to fast on these days is a violation. The commentary also clarifies exceptions: if someone mistakenly thought a fast was obligatory and then discovered it was not, and broke immediately, no qada; but if he delayed, qada becomes due. There is also a separate dispute regarding a woman who begins a voluntary fast and then menstruates: the correct view is that qada is required.

Key Terms
التطوع (al-tatawwu'): Voluntary, supererogatory acts of worship (nafl).
أيام التشريق (ayyam al-tashriq): The three days following Eid al-Adha: 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul-Hijjah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast was never valid because it was started without intention.
B) The very act of beginning the fast on Eid is a sin, so there is no obligation to complete or make up.
C) The person can simply continue the fast without breaking it.
D) The Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly exempted these days.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school internally disagrees on whether breaking a voluntary fast on the five prohibited days requires qada: Abu Hanifa says no, the Sahibayn say yes, and later practice often follows the Sahibayn.

Qada for Breaking a Voluntary Fast: The Dispute on Prohibited Days
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada for broken fast on prohibited days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi ruling on qada for a broken voluntary fast and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the five prohibited days, including the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Breaking a voluntary fast generally requires qada, but according to Abu Hanifa, no qada is due on the five prohibited days because the commencement itself is disobedience.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah addresses the obligation of making up a voluntary (nafl) fast if one breaks it. While the general rule is that qada is required to preserve the act from nullification, an exception is made for the five days when fasting is prohibited. The text presents the view of Imam Abu Hanifa and the differing opinion of his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, with a detailed rationale rooted in the nature of commencement and obedience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
Read the passage to find the statement that a voluntary faster who breaks his fast must make it up (qada). Note that the text says 'there is no disagreement among our companions' regarding this obligation.
This is the default rule: breaking any voluntary fast requires qada, regardless of the reason.
2
Recognize the Exception
Find the exception: if the voluntary fast was begun on the five prohibited days (the two Eids and the three days of Tashriq). According to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) from Abu Hanifa, no qada is required.
The exception is only for these specific days; other days are not exempt.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's Reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that fasting on these days is prohibited by divine command. By commencing such a fast, the person is engaging in disobedience from the very start. The act is commanded to be cut off, so there is no obligation to complete it, and consequently no qada.
Key point: commencement itself is a sin; thus there is no 'preservation' of a valid act.
4
Compare with the Sahibayn's View
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that qada is required even on these days. They draw an analogy (qiyas) with voluntary prayer in disliked times: if one begins such a prayer, the commencement is binding (like a vow), and qada is required if broken.
The Sahibayn see commencement as creating an obligation, regardless of the initial prohibition.

Source Text
