For a human need, or a necessary need, such as the collapse of a mosque, or performing testimony that has become obligatory upon him, or being forcibly expelled by a tyrant, or the dispersal of his family due to the loss of what was intended from it, or fear for himself or his property from oppressors. So he enters another mosque immediately, intending that his exit is only to perform i'tikaf in another mosque, and he occupies himself only with going to the other mosque. But if he leaves for an hour without a valid excuse, the obligatory (i'tikaf) is invalidated, and there is no sin upon him. And it is invalidated by unconsciousness and madness if they continue for days, except the first day if he remains and completes it in the mosque. He makes up what remains after the madness subsides, and unconsciousness even if prolonged like madness by istihsan. And the two (Sahibayn) said: If he leaves for more than half the day, it is invalidated; otherwise not. And it ended with him meaning by exiting non-obligatory i'tikaf, which is supererogatory, for it has no limit. Then the commentary: 'Or a necessary need' etc. Al-Sayyid said in his commentary: Know that what the author mentioned of i'tikaf not being invalidated by exiting due to the mosque collapsing and the subsequent excuses he mentioned is the school of the Sahibayn. As for Imam Abu Hanifa, it is invalidated because the excuse in these matters is not of frequent occurrence. And in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: As for what does not frequently occur, like rescuing a drowning person or a mosque collapsing, it removes sin but not invalidity; otherwise, forgetfulness would be more deserving of non-invalidity as verified by al-Kamal, contrary to what al-Zayla'i and others detailed. But in al-Nahr and others, they made non-invalidity for its collapse and the invalidity of his congregation and his forced expulsion as istihsan. And his statement: 'And performing testimony that has become obligatory upon him' includes that this is from the shari'i needs. His statement: 'for the loss of what was intended from it' is the cause in these matters, meaning that his i'tikaf is not invalidated, but rather he goes out to another because the intended purpose, which is performing prayer in that mosque in the most complete manner, has been lost. His statement: 'from oppressors' meaning the arrogant ones from 'kibr' meaning arrogance. His statement: 'intending' that it not be... meaning that the intention of the hour is not actually intended due to the possibility of distance between the two mosques. His statement: 'without a valid excuse' meaning regarding non-invalidity, so if he leaves for a prohibited funeral or his wife, it is invalidated because even though it is an excuse, it is not considered in the invalidity. His statement: 'and there is no sin upon him' meaning due to the excuse; otherwise, without excuse he sins because of His statement: 'And do not invalidate your deeds' (Surah Muhammad: 47). His statement: 'if it continues' meaning each of them. His statement: 'and completes it in the mosque' — if he leaves it, then he must also make it up due to the absence of the pillar. His statement: 'and he makes up what remains after the subsidence' meaning by fasting when able, as compensation for what he missed, except that if the vowed i'tikaf was for a specific month, he makes up only the amount that was invalidated, and he is not required to start over, as in the fast of Ramadan. But if the vowed i'tikaf was for a non-specific month, he must start over because it became binding continuously, so the attribute of continuity is observed in it. Its completion is in al-Bahr. His statement: 'And the two said: if he leaves more than half the day...' They said: This is istihsan, which necessitates giving preference to their statement. Al-Bahr, and al-Kamal discussed it and gave preference to his (Imam's) statement because the necessity upon which alleviation is based must be continuous and predominating, and it is not so here. Thus it is from the cases where analogical reasoning is applied, as in Tuhfat al-Akhyar.
Source pages p. 703

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi discusses the conditions under which a person may leave the mosque during obligatory i'tikaf without invalidating it. The text first lists valid excuses given by the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani): collapse of the mosque, obligatory testimony, forcible expulsion, loss of family, fear of oppressors. If these occur, the person may leave and immediately go to another mosque to continue i'tikaf, provided the exit is solely for that purpose. If he leaves without a valid excuse for any amount of time, the obligatory i'tikaf is invalidated, but he has no sin if he had an excuse. The passage then discusses invalidation by unconsciousness or madness lasting days; the first day is not counted if he returns and completes it. The Sahibayn hold that leaving for more than half the day invalidates i'tikaf, while Imam Abu Hanifa considers any exit without a valid excuse invalidating. The hashya notes that this difference stems from whether excuses that are not predominant (ghayr ghalib) can prevent invalidation. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that such excuses are rare and thus do not prevent invalidation, while the Sahibayn use istihsan (juristic preference) based on necessity. The text also clarifies that if one leaves for a prohibited purpose or for his wife, it invalidates even if it seems an excuse. Finally, it discusses how to make up invalidated i'tikaf: if it was a specific month, only the missed days are made up without restarting; if not specific, the entire i'tikaf must be repeated continuously.

Key Terms
اعتكاف (i'tikaf): Seclusion in a mosque with the intention of worship, specifically fasting and staying in the mosque.
صاحبين (Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the primary disciples of Abu Hanifa.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides strict analogy (qiyas) based on custom, necessity, or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what is the ruling if a person leaves obligatory i'tikaf due to the collapse of the mosque?
A) The i'tikaf is invalidated and must be restarted from the beginning.
B) The i'tikaf is not invalidated and the person may continue in another mosque.
C) The i'tikaf is invalidated but no sin is incurred.
D) The i'tikaf is valid only if the collapse was predicted.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, exiting obligatory i'tikaf for valid necessities (like mosque collapse) does not invalidate it, based on istihsan, and this is the relied-upon fatwa.

Rulings on Actions During I'tikaf
Rulings on eating, sleeping, buying, and silence in i'tikaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi rulings on permitted, disliked, and prohibited actions during i'tikaf and explain the evidence (dalil) and reasoning behind each ruling.

Core Idea
I'tikaf is total devotion; trade and silence as worship violate it, while necessary acts are permitted.

This lesson examines the rulings governing what a person in i'tikaf (mu'takif) may or may not do within the mosque. The Hanafi school balances the need for essential activities with the sacred nature of i'tikaf, declaring some actions permissible, others disliked, and some forbidden. Understanding these distinctions helps preserve the spiritual purpose of i'tikaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each action mentioned (eating, drinking, sleeping, buying, selling, silence, cleaning), determine the hukm level: permissible, disliked (makruh tahrimi), or forbidden (haram).
The text explicitly states the ruling for each.
2
Examine exceptions and conditions
Note when an action becomes permissible despite a general prohibition, e.g., leaving the mosque after sunset for food if no one can bring it, or bringing food items vs. trade items.
Necessity (darurah) can override dislike.
3
Analyze the evidence (dalil)
The text cites a hadith listing actions not befitting the mosque, including 'setting up a market'. Understand how this hadith supports the ruling against trade in the mosque.
The hadith is from Ibn Majah; it is zanni al-thubut but used as evidence.
4
Understand the rationale
I'tikaf requires cutting off worldly engagements; the mosque is dedicated to worship. Bringing trade items or conducting commerce contradicts this purpose.
Silence as worship is prohibited because it resembles the practice of the People of the Book and was abrogated.
5
Compare with non-mu'takif
The text notes differences for those not in i'tikaf, e.g., sleeping in the mosque is permissible for a stranger, and buying/selling is disliked but not forbidden.
This shows that the heightened restrictions apply specifically to the mu'takif.

الأشياء بالمعتكف لأنّ غيره يكره له المبايعة فيه مطلقاً والأكل، والنوم قيل : إلا لغريب كما في الأشباه وفي المجتبى ولغير المعتكف أن ينام في المسجد مقيماً كان أو غريباً مضطجعاً، أو متكناً رجلاه إلى الإسلام سهلاً لا حرج فيه قوله : (وقيل : يخرج بعد الغروب للأكل والشرب) قال في البحر ينبغي . على ما إذا لم يجد من يأتي له به فحينئذ يكون من الحوائج الضرورية ا هـ . قوله: (وكره إحضار المبيع فيه أي تحريماً لأنها محل إطلاقهم بحر. قوله : (لأن المسجد محرر) أي مخلص، وفي نسخة بالزاي آخره أي محفوظ ، لأن فيه شغله، ولهذا قالوا لا يجوز غرس الأشجار فيه قلت والظاهر أنه لا يكره إحضار المأكول لأنه يتناوله فيه، ومثله المشروب فتحمل الكراهة على ما لا يحتاجه لنفسه فيه وفي الحموي عن البرجندي إحضار الثمن، أو المبيع الذي لا يشغل في المسجد جائز . قوله : ( وكره عقد ما كان للتجارة) وإن لم يحضر المبيع فيه قوله : ( ولهذا كره الخياطة ونحوها كبيع وشراء وتعليم كتابة بأجر وكل شيء يكره فيه يكره في سطحه كذا في البحر. قوله : (مطلقاً) أي سواء حضر المبيع أم لا احتاج إليه أم لا كان للتجارة أم لا كما يفاد من البحر. قوله: (وكره الصمت الخ) سئل الإمام عن بيانه فقال : يصوم، ولا يكلم أحداً، ولم يبق صوم . الصمت قربة في شريعتنا فإنه منهي عنه. قوله : فلا بأس به) المراد به أنه مطلوب شرعاً، ولما كان يتوهم منه أنه مساو لغيره من And the eating, drinking, sleeping, and concluding a sale for what he needs for himself or his family of the person in i'tikaf: there is no invalidity for the one in i'tikaf. The commentary: His statement: 'And the eating of the person in i'tikaf, etc.' And he may wash his head in the mosque if it does not stain it with used water; if it stains, it is prevented because it would not be except in the mosque due to the necessity of i'tikaf. Even if he leaves for these things, his i'tikaf is invalidated. And in al-Zahiriyyah: It is said he may go out after sunset to eat and drink. And it is disliked (makruh) to bring the sold item into the mosque because the mosque is freed from the rights of people, so one should not make it like a shop. And it is disliked to contract for trade because he is devoted to Allah, so he should not engage in worldly matters; for this reason sewing and the like are disliked in it. And it is disliked for non-sale absolutely. And it is disliked to remain silent if he believes it is worship, because it is prohibited, as it is the fast of the People of the Book and has been abrogated. But if he does not believe it is worship, but keeps his tongue from speaking what is useless, then there is no harm, but he should adhere to reciting Quran, dhikr, hadith, knowledge and its study, and the biography of the Prophet and stories. The person in i'tikaf may carry cleaning the mosque it is obligatory. And if he performs wudu in the mosque in a vessel, it follows this detail. Unlike the non-mu'takif, it is disliked for him to perform wudu in the mosque even in a vessel unless it is in a place prepared for that, no prayer there. And in al-Fath: There are traits that should not be in the mosque: it should not be taken as a thoroughfare, weapons should not be brandished, a bow should not be strung, arrows should not be set, raw meat should not be passed, a hadd punishment should not be carried out, and a market should not be set up – Ibn Majah narrated it in al-Sunan. His statement: 'his i'tikaf is invalidated due to lack of necessity.' And these matters are restricted to the mu'takif because for others it is disliked to transact absolutely and to eat and sleep; it is said except for a stranger as in al-Ashbah. And in al-Mujtaba: For the non-mu'takif, it is permissible to sleep in the mosque, whether resident or stranger, lying down or reclining with his feet towards the qibla or otherwise; so the mu'takif is more deserving. But his statement 'feet towards the qibla' is debatable because they ruled it disliked. So the conclusion is that there are two opinions regarding these actions in the mosque for the non-mu'takif. His statement: 'and it is said: he may go out after sunset to eat and drink.' Al-Bahr said: It should be when he cannot find someone to bring it to him, then it becomes a necessary need. His statement: 'and it is disliked to bring the sold item into the mosque' meaning prohibitively disliked because it is a place of [؟]. Al-Bahr: 'because the mosque is freed' meaning dedicated; in another version with 'z' meaning preserved, because it is occupied. For this reason they said: planting trees in it is not permissible. I say: It appears that there is no dislike in bringing food, because he consumes it there, and similarly drink. So the dislike is applied to what he does not need for himself there. In al-Hamawi from al-Barjandi: Bringing the price or the sold item that does not occupy the mosque is permissible. His statement: 'and it is disliked to contract for trade' even if the sold item is not brought into the mosque. His statement: 'and for this reason sewing and the like are disliked' like buying and selling and teaching writing for a fee. And everything disliked in the mosque is also disliked on its roof. Al-Bahr. His statement: 'absolutely' meaning whether the sold item is present or not, whether he needs it or not, whether it is for trade or not, as understood from al-Bahr. His statement: 'and it is disliked to remain silent' – Imam was asked about its explanation; he said: He fasts and does not speak to anyone, but silence is not a worship in our Shari'ah because it is prohibited. His statement: 'so there is no harm' means it is legally required? Actually it means it is permissible, but because it might be thought that it is equivalent to other acts like recitation, he said 'rather he should adhere' meaning he should spend most of his time in that. His statement: 'and dhikr' is what...
Source pages p. 703

Commentary
This passage covers the rulings on actions that a person in i'tikaf (mu'takif) may perform within the mosque. The base text states that eating, drinking, sleeping, and concluding a sale for necessary needs do not invalidate i'tikaf. The commentary expands: the mu'takif may wash his head if it does not stain the mosque; otherwise it is prevented because the mosque must be kept clean. Leaving the mosque for these actions invalidates i'tikaf, but there is an exception for eating and drinking after sunset if no one can bring it. The text then discusses disliked (makruh) actions: bringing the sold item into the mosque is makruh tahriman because the mosque is dedicated to worship, not worldly transactions. Contracting for trade (even without bringing the item) is also disliked. Sewing and similar worldly activities are disliked. Silence, if believed to be an act of worship, is prohibited (haram) because it resembles the practice of Jews and was abrogated; however, refraining from useless speech without believing it is worship is permissible, but one should engage in recitation and learning. The passage also notes that cleaning the mosque is obligatory for the mu'takif. For non-mu'takif, many of these actions are less restricted; for example, sleeping in the mosque is permissible for a stranger. The commentary cites a hadith listing actions that should not be done in the mosque: using it as a thoroughfare, brandishing weapons, etc. Finally, the text clarifies that the dislike of bringing items into the mosque is specifically for worldly trade items, not for food or necessary items. The overall emphasis is on keeping the mosque dedicated to worship and avoiding anything that turns it into a marketplace or place of worldly engagement.

Key Terms
كراهة تحريمية (karahah tahrimeeyah): A ruling close to haram; doing it is sinful but the act is not invalid.
صمت (samt): Silence; when believed to be an act of worship, it is prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a person in i'tikaf remaining silent with the intention of it being an act of worship?
A) It is recommended (mandub) because silence helps concentration.
B) It is prohibited (haram) because it resembles the practice of the People of the Book.
C) It is permissible (mubah) as long as one does not disturb others.
D) It is disliked (makruh tanzihi) because it wastes time that could be spent in dhikr.

Key Takeaway
I'tikaf demands dedication to worship; trade and intentionally silent devotion violate its spirit, while essential acts are allowed with conditions.

Invalidation of I'tikaf and Inclusion of Nights
Explanation of i'tikaf invalidation and binding of nights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why intercourse invalidates i'tikaf even if forgetful, and apply the Hanafi rule that vowing days of i'tikaf in the plural or dual form includes the corresponding nights.

Core Idea
I'tikaf is invalidated by intercourse even forgetfully due to its 'reminding state', like prayer and Hajj, unlike fasting.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah clarifies two distinct rulings on i'tikaf. First, it establishes that sexual intercourse breaks i'tikaf regardless of intent, night or day, because the act itself carries a reminder. Second, it addresses the binding nature of nights when one vows days of i'tikaf, deriving inclusion from the plural form and applying precaution to the dual. An intra-Hanafi difference from Abu Yusuf nuances the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and invalidation rule
Determine what specific act (intercourse) invalidates i'tikaf, and whether the ruling changes based on intention or time.
The text covers intentional, forgetful, and coerced intercourse.
2
Analyze the ta'lil (legal reasoning)
Understand why intercourse invalidates i'tikaf even forgetfully by focusing on the 'reminding state' (hāla mudhakkir), and contrast with fasting.
The reminding state exists because the person is in the mosque, engaged in worship.
3
Examine the ruling on nocturnal emission
Determine that nocturnal emission from thinking or looking does not invalidate i'tikaf, as it is not directly caused by a prohibited act.
No reminder is needed; it is an involuntary emission.
4
Apply the inclusive principle to vowed days
Analyze how mentioning 'days' in the plural includes the nights, citing Quranic usage and the inherent continuity of i'tikaf.
The dual form is treated analogously by precaution.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare the majority position with Abu Yusuf's view that the first night is not binding, and note the thamarah al-khilaf.
Abu Yusuf's reasoning: night i'tikaf is only subsidiary to connecting days.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

في الحج التجرد عن اللباس، وتجنب الطيب قوله (والحج) فإنه يبطل إحرامه بالوطء، وبالإنزال بدواعيه، ولو كان ناسياً بخلاف الصوم فإنه لا يبطل بفعل ذلك ناسياً لعدم المذكر قوله : ولزمته الليالي الخ) وذلك لأن ذكر أحد اللفظين بلفظ الجمع يدخل ما بإزائها من الآخر قال تعالى : (ثلاثة أيام إلا رمزاً وقال تعالى: (ثلاث ليال سوياً القصة واحدة فعبر عنها تارة بالأيام، وتارة بالليالي فعلم أن ذكر أحدهما بلفظ الجمع يتناول الآخر وحاصله أنه إما أن يأتي بلفظ المفرد، أو المثنى، أو المجموع وكل منها ما أن يكون في الأيام، أو الليالي فهي ستة وفي كل منها أما أن ينوي الحقيقة أو المجاز أو ينوبهما ، أو لم تكن له نية فهي أربعة وعشرون وحكم الجميع مذكور في البحر. قوله : (متتابعة) حال . من الأيام، وحذف نظيره من الجملة السابقة. قوله : ( وتأثيره) لو قال وضابطه لكان أوضح وتوضيحه ما في السيد عن البحر حيث قال لأن الإطلاق في الاعتكاف كالتصريح بالتتابع بخلاف الإطلاق في نذر الصوم، والفرق أن الاعتكاف يدوم بالليل والنهار بخلاف الصوم، فإنه لا يوجد ليلا ا هـ فالمتفرق في نفسه لأنه يتخلل فيه زمن ليس محللاً له، وهو الليل، والمتصل Or forgetfully or coerced, by night or by day, because it (intercourse) has a reminding state like prayer and Hajj, unlike fasting. And if he has a nocturnal emission due to thinking or looking, his i'tikaf is not invalidated. And the nights are also binding upon him, i.e., as the days are binding when he vows to observe days of i'tikaf, because mentioning days in the plural form includes their corresponding nights. The first night enters, so he enters the mosque before sunset on the first night and leaves after sunset on the last day. And the days are binding when he vows by nights consecutively, even if he did not stipulate consecutiveness in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) because consecutiveness is inherent. Its effect is that what is inherently separate does not require connection except by stipulation, and what is inherently connected cannot be separated except by stipulation. And two nights are binding when he vows two days, so he enters at sunset as we mentioned, because the dual is in the meaning of the plural, so it is attached to it here by way of precaution. He said: (Or coerced) the first is 'or coerced'. He said: (because it has a reminding state) which is being in the mosque, and his saying: like prayer, the reminder in it is being in ihram, reciting, facing the qiblah, and the reminder in Hajj is disrobing from sewn clothes and avoiding perfume. He said: (and Hajj) for his ihram is invalidated by intercourse and by ejaculation due to its causes, even if forgetfully, unlike fasting, for it is not invalidated by doing that forgetfully due to the absence of a reminder. He said: (and the nights also) meaning because mentioning one of the two words (days/nights) in the plural form includes the corresponding other. Allah said: 'Three days except for gesture' and said: 'Three nights soundly' the story is one, so it was expressed sometimes by days and sometimes by nights. So it is known that mentioning one of them in the plural form includes the other. Its summary is: either he comes with a singular, dual, or plural, and each is either for days or nights. That is six. And in each, either he intends the literal meaning, metaphorical meaning, both, or no intention. That is twenty-four. The ruling of all is mentioned in al-Bahr. He said: (consecutively) is a condition from the days, and its counterpart is omitted from the preceding sentence. He said: (and its effect) if he said 'and its rule' it would be clearer. Its clarification is what is in al-Sayyid from al-Bahr, where he said: because the absolute in i'tikaf is like explicit stipulation of consecutiveness, unlike the absolute in vows of fasting. The difference is that i'tikaf continues by night and day, unlike fasting, for it does not exist at night. So what is inherently separate is that a time that is not a locus for it intervenes, which is the night. And what is inherently connected is i'tikaf, because it encompasses night and day. He said: (as we mentioned) i.e., in the plural. He said: (because the dual is in the meaning of the plural) and from Abu Yusuf, in the dual and plural, the first night is not binding, because i'tikaf at night is only as a consequence of the necessity of connecting the days, and there is no need to include the first night to achieve connection without it. And some have made the disagreement of Abu Yusuf only in the dual (Zayla'i).
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hāshiya) from al-Tahtawi on Marāqī al-Falāḥ, explaining two key rulings on i'tikāf. First, it addresses the invalidation of i'tikāf by sexual intercourse: whether done intentionally, forgetfully, or under coercion, both by night and day, it breaks the i'tikāf. The reasoning (ta'līl) is that intercourse has a 'reminding state' (hāla mudhakkir) that prevents forgetfulness, similar to actions in prayer and Hajj. In contrast, fasting is not invalidated by intercourse performed forgetfully, because fasting lacks such a constant reminder. Second, the passage discusses nocturnal emission: if it occurs due to thinking or looking (without physical touch), it does not invalidate i'tikāf. Then the text transitions to the ruling on the binding nature of nights when one vows days of i'tikāf. The key principle is that when one uses a plural form (e.g., 'days'), the corresponding nights are automatically included, based on the Quranic usage where 'days' and 'nights' are used interchangeably for the same period. This means that if one vows to observe i'tikāf for a number of days, the nights between them are also required, and he must enter the mosque before sunset on the first night and leave after sunset on the last day. Similarly, if one vows nights, the days are also binding, and the sequence must be consecutive even without stipulation, because i'tikāf inherently extends over both day and night, unlike fasting which exists only by day. The commentator notes that for a dual form (e.g., 'two days'), two nights are also required by analogy to the plural, as a precaution. An intra-Hanafī difference is reported from Abu Yusuf: he holds that the first night is not binding in either dual or plural, because night-time i'tikāf is only a necessary consequence of connecting days, and the first night is not needed for that connection. However, the relied-upon position (muftā bihi) is that nights are binding.

Key Terms
حالة مذكرة (hāla mudhakkir): A state that serves as a reminder, preventing forgetfulness, e.g., being in the mosque for i'tikāf, or being in ihram for Hajj.
مثنى (muthannā): The dual form in Arabic grammar; here it refers to vowing 'two days' which triggers the same ruling as the plural regarding inclusion of nights.
تبعاً (taba'an): As a consequence or subsidiary; Abu Yusuf argues that night i'tikāf is only subsidiary to day i'tikāf for connecting days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) His i'tikāf is not invalidated
C) He must perform a make-up i'tikāf
D) He must make wudū' but i'tikāf remains valid

Key Takeaway
I'tikaf is invalidated by intercourse even forgetfully due to the reminding state, and vowing days of i'tikaf in plural or dual form automatically includes the nights.

Intention for Days Only in I'tikaf and Vowing a Month
Ruling on intention for days only in i'tikaf and linguistic analysis of 'month'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of intending only days in i'tikaf and explain why a month vow automatically includes nights.

Core Idea
For i'tikaf vows, 'days' can exclude nights if less than a month, but 'month' always includes nights unless excepted.

This lesson examines a nuanced fiqh ruling regarding vows of i'tikaf. When someone vows to observe i'tikaf for a certain number of days, can they intend only the daytime? The answer depends on whether the period is less than a month or a full month. The passage also clarifies the linguistic basis for this distinction, touching on usul al-fiqh concepts of khass (specific) and amm (general).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making a vow (nadhr) to perform i'tikaf. The ruling concerns the validity of intending to limit the i'tikaf to days only, excluding nights.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites the Quranic verse (al-Baqarah 2:187) about i'tikaf in mosques, which serves as the primary evidence for the rulings of i'tikaf.
The verse is quoted: 'ولا تباشروهن وأنتم عاكفون في المساجد'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse establishes the permissibility and validity conditions of i'tikaf. The ruling on intention is derived from general principles of vows and the literal meaning of terms.
The connection is that the verse shows i'tikaf is valid only in mosques and sets the framework; specific rulings on intention come from hadith and linguistic analysis.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text mentions that the ruling is mukhtalaf fih (disputed) across schools. The Hanafi position is stricter for a month, while other schools may allow more flexibility.
The cross-madhhab status is noted as mukhtalaf_fih.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that for vows less than a month, intending only days is valid. For a month, nights are automatically included unless explicitly excepted.
Al-Tahtawi cites al-Fath al-Qadir and al-Inaya as sources for this tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح نية النهر) جمع نهار (خاصة) بالاعتكاف إذا نوى تخصيصه بالأيام دون (الليالي إذا نذر اعتكاف دون شهر لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه فتعمل نيته كقوله نذرت اعتكاف عشرين يوماً، ونوى بياض النهار يخص النهار . قوله : (إلا) أن يصرح بالاستثناء) مراده به ما يعم التقييد ليعم ما لو قال: شهر بالنهار دون الليالي. قوله : (لأن الشهر اسم لمقدر الخ) أي فهو خاص وهو كل لفظ وضع لمعنى على الانفراد قوله: (وليس باسم عام كالعشرة) فيه أن العشرة من أسماء العدد وهي من الخاص قال في شرح المنار كصاحب البحر : والمراد بقوله : أي في تعريف الخاص على الانفراد أن لا يكون لذلك المعنى الواحد أفراد سواء كان له أجزاء، أو لم يكن فتدخل التثنية كما في التلويح ، واسم العدد تحت الخاص كالمائة فإنّ الواضع وضعه لمجموع وحدان الكثير من حيث هو مجموع ، فيكون كل من الوحد أن جزاً من أجزائه، فيكون موضوعاً لواحد بالنوع كالرجل والفرس بخلاف العام، فإنه موضوع لأمر يشترك فيه وحد أن الكثير فيكون كل من الواحد أن جزئياً من جزئياته، وبخلاف المشترك فإنّ كلا من الوحدان نفس الموضوع له كما في التلويح لكن ظاهر ما في التوضيح، والتلويح، والتحرير أن العدد موضوع لكثير كالعام فالمسمى متعدد فيهما لكن الأول محصور، والثاني لا ا هـ قلت: ويمكن الجمع بأنّ اسم العدد كالعشرة بالنظر إلى كونه لا يشمل الزائد عنها، أو الناقص خاص، وبالنظر إلى كونه يصدق على كل عشرة عام فتأمل. قوله : (على مجموع الأحاد) فيه أن شهر السم لمجموع الليل والنهار في المدة المعينة فهما سواء ويدل له قوله كما لا تنطلق العشرة الخ. قوله : (ولا) (مجازاً) فيه أن يقال ما المانع من إطلاق الشهر مثلاً على النهار مجازاً من إطلاق اسم الكل على جزئه قوله : (بعد الثنيا) أي الاستثناء، والمراد بعد المستثنى. قوله : (الليالي المجردة) خبر أن قوله : هذا من فتح القدير) أراد أن هذا الكلام منقول من الفتح والعناية وأراد المعنى اللغوي أيضاً. قوله: (فالإضافة إلى المساجد) مراده
He said: (And the intention of the day (nahar) is valid) plural of nahaar (daytime) specifically for i'tikaf if he intends to limit it to the days without the nights when he vows i'tikaf for less than a month, because he intended the reality of his speech, so his intention is effective, like his saying 'I vow i'tikaf for twenty days' and he intends the whiteness of the day, he specifies the day. He said: (except that he explicitly makes an exception) meaning he includes all constraints to include if he says 'a month by day without nights'. He said: (because the month is a name for an estimated amount) i.e., it is specific, and it is every word placed for a meaning individually. He said: (and it is not a common noun like ten) therein that ten is a number and is specific. The author of Sharh al-Manar like the author of al-Bahr said: The meaning of his saying, i.e., in the definition of specific individually, is that that single meaning has no individuals, whether it has parts or not, so the dual is included as in al-Talwih. And the number name is under the specific like one hundred, because the word setter placed it for a collection of many units as a whole, so each unit is a part of its parts, so it is placed for one type like 'man' and 'horse', unlike the common noun, for it is placed for an attribute shared by many units, so each unit is a particular of its particulars, and unlike the shared term (mushtarak), for each of the units is the same thing placed for it as in al-Talwih. But what appears in al-Tawdih, al-Talwih, and al-Tahrir is that the number is placed for many like the common noun, so the named is multiple in both, but the first is restricted and the second is not. End quote. I say: And it is possible to reconcile that the number name like ten is specific from the perspective that it does not include more or less, and from the perspective that it applies to every ten it is general, so ponder. He said: (on the collection of units) therein that the name 'month' is for the collection of night and day in a determined period, so they are equal. And evidence for it is his statement 'just as ten does not apply' etc. He said: (and not) (metaphorically) therein it can be said: what prevents using 'month' for the daytime metaphorically, as a use of the whole for a part? Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This section of the super-commentary clarifies the rulings on intention (niyya) in vows of i'tikāf. The first ruling: if a person vows to observe i'tikāf for a period of days and intends only the daytime (nahār) to be included, not the nights, this intention is valid provided the vowed period is less than a month. This is because the word 'days' (ayyām) can be interpreted literally as daytime only, and his intention aligns with that literal meaning, so it is effective. For example, vowing 'twenty days' and intending only the bright daytime hours. However, if the vow is for a month (shahr), the intention cannot exclude the nights, because 'month' legislatively and linguistically encompasses both day and night as a continuous period. One must either explicitly state an exception (e.g., 'a month by day without nights') or use a dual expression. The text discusses the nature of 'special' (khāss) versus 'general' ('āmm) terms, using the example of numbers like 'ten', which are special in that they refer to a specific quantity, not an open-ended set. The month is similarly a specific measure of time that includes both day and night. The author also notes that the legal basis of i'tikāf is derived from the Quranic verse (al-Baqarah 2:187): 'Do not have sexual relations with them while you are in retreat in the mosques.' The mention of 'mosques' indicates the specific virtue of mosques and the permissibility of leaving permissible acts like intercourse during i'tikāf.

Key Terms
شهر (shahr): A month, defined as a specific period comprising both days and nights, like a lunar month.
خاص (khāss): A specific term that denotes a single meaning without individuals, e.g., 'ten' refers to a specific number, not an open-ended set.
استثناء (istithnā'): An exception that excludes part of the subject from the ruling; here, explicit exception is required to exclude nights from a month vow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a person vows 'a month of i'tikāf' and intends only the days without the nights?
A) The intention is valid and only the days are binding.
B) The intention is not valid and both days and nights are binding.
C) The intention is valid only if he adds 'by day' explicitly.
D) The entire vow is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of intending only days in i'tikaf depends on the vow's wording: for less than a month, it is valid; for a month, nights are automatically included unless explicitly excepted.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
10 min
Ruling and Virtues of I'tikaf in Ramadan
Ruling and virtues of i'tikaf with proof and rational arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on i'tikaf, cite the primary hadith proof, and identify the key virtues that make it a recommended practice.

Core Idea
I'tikaf is a confirmed communal sunnah, proven by the Prophet's continuous practice and its profound spiritual benefits.

This passage establishes i'tikaf (seclusion in the mosque for worship) in the last ten days of Ramadan as an emphasized sunnah (sunnah muakkadah). It provides both transmitted (hadith) and rational arguments for its virtue. The author explains why i'tikaf is among the noblest deeds: it empties the heart from worldly concerns, allows one to focus solely on worship, and offers protection from spiritual enemies. This lesson walks through the proofs and the Hanafi classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what i'tikaf is and its hukm level according to the Hanafi school.
The passage states it is 'sunnah kifayah' (communal sunnah) and 'muakkadah' (emphasized).
2
Locate the primary transmitted proof
Find the hadith that establishes the ruling. The passage cites the report of Abu Huraira and Aisha (may Allah be pleased with them).
The hadith states the Prophet (peace be upon him) observed i'tikaf in the last ten days from his arrival in Medina until his death.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves a sunnah, not an obligation. The Prophet's continuous practice (كان يعتكف) indicates emphasis, but his occasional omission (due to the tent incident) shows it is not wajib.
Al-Tahtawi mentions that the Prophet once missed i'tikaf and made it up later, reinforcing that it is a sunnah.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize that some scholars held i'tikaf as mustahabb, but the relied-upon position (al-mu'tamad) is sunnah muakkadah.
The author states 'ala al-mu'tamad' to indicate the preferred view.

Book of Fasting: For it is evidence that it is an act of devotion (and the Sunnah) because it is related on the authority of Abu Huraira and Aisha, may Allah be pleased with them, that: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, used to observe i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan from the time he came to Medina until Allah took him.' And al-Zuhri, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'I am amazed at people how they have abandoned i'tikaf, while the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, would do something and leave it, and he never abandoned i'tikaf until he passed away.' And he indicated its establishment by a type of rational argument and said: 'And it is among the noblest deeds if it is with sincerity to Allah, because the one performing i'tikaf is waiting for prayer and is like one praying, and that state is one of proximity and detachment, and its virtues cannot be counted. Among its virtues is that it empties the heart from worldly matters by occupying it with turning to worship, single-mindedly devoting oneself to it, and surrendering the soul to the Lord' by entrusting its affairs to the Mighty Court, relying on His generosity, standing at His door, adhering to His worship, and seeking nearness to Him in hope of His mercy, as alluded to in the hadith of 'whoever draws near' and adhering to the decision (in His house) - glorified and exalted be He - befitting the Owner of the house to honor His guest as a favor, mercy, and kindness from Him, and a grace for seeking refuge in Him (and seeking fortification in His fortress, so that his enemy cannot reach him with his cunning and overpowering, due to the strength of Allah's authority and His subduing and mighty support. That [adding] its occurrence in them. His saying: (the specific) describes the mosques. His saying: (and abandonment) is raised, conjoined to addition. His saying: (for it) meaning i'tikaf; indeed the prohibition of conjugal relations is restricted by it in the verse. His saying: (and the Sunnah) it has preceded that it is a communal sunnah, and it is emphasized according to the relied-upon position, and there is no contradiction between its emphasis and being communal, and it was said it is recommended in the last ten days. His saying: (amazement) is an absolute object for an omitted verb, meaning 'I am amazed with amazement.' His saying: (and he never abandoned i'tikaf) meaning in the last ten days until he passed away, i.e., except for an excuse, because it is related that he performed i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan and saw tents and domes set up in the mosque. He said: 'Whose are these?' They said: 'This is for Aisha, this is for Hafsa, and this is for Saudah.' So the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, became angry and said: 'Do you consider righteousness to be this?' So he ordered that his own tent be removed, and it was removed, and he did not perform i'tikaf then, and later he made it up in Shawwal. His saying: ((by a type)) i.e., a kind, and his saying: of rational argument i.e., from rational evidence. His saying: (and he is like one praying) i.e., the one waiting is given the reward of the one praying, as reported in the narration. His saying: (and it) i.e., the prayer. His saying: (and detachment) i.e., from worldly diversions. His saying: (and its virtues cannot be counted) i.e., the prayer or the state. His saying: (by occupying) is attached to 'emptying', and the ba indicates causality. His saying: (single-mindedly for it) is an independent circumstantial clause; if he does not single-mindedly devote himself, his heart is not free. His saying: (by entrusting its affairs) the ba is for depiction. His saying: (to the Mighty Court) al-janab means courtyard, dwelling, direction, and mountain, as said the traditionist in al-Qamus. His saying: (and standing at his door) contains a figurative simile. His saying: (and adhering to His worship) i.e., expressions of his saying 'by occupying with turning' etc. His saying: (and seeking nearness to Him) with jarr conjoined to His worship, and with nasb conjoined to emptying; and the meaning is seeking nearness to Him through worship. His saying: (in the hadith of 'whoever draws near') its completion is 'a cubit, I draw near to him a fathom.' She informed me (1) His saying: 'and it' i.e., the connection in a copy meaning i'tikaf, and you are considering the report. Book of Fasting, its service, 709, and his victory. You see subjects confine themselves at the door of their sultan while he is an individual among them, and they strive in humble standing before him to fulfill their needs, so he turns to them with his goodness, and protects them from their enemy with the might of his power and the strength of his authority. And he indicated attaining the objective, and removed the veil of doubt and lifted the covering, and manifested the truth by an outpouring of giving, by what he alluded to in his saying: (And said) the master, the knower of Allah, the Imam, the mujtahid (Ata) ibn Abi Rabah, the tabi'i, disciple of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them), one of the teachers of the Great Imam (may Allah have mercy on him). Abu Hanifa said: 'I have not seen anyone more knowledgeable in fiqh than Hammad, nor anyone more comprehensive in sciences than Ata ibn Abi Rabah.' Most of the narrations of the Great Imam Abu Hanifa are from Ata; he heard from Ibn Abbas, Ibn Umar, Abu Huraira, Abu Sa'id, Jabir, and Aisha (may Allah be pleased with them). He died in the year 115 AH at the age of eighty, as stated in I'lam al-Akhyar. He (may Allah have mercy on him and benefit us with his blessing and support) said: (The similitude of the one performing i'tikaf is like a man who goes back and forth) i.e., hesitates and stands at the door of a king or a great minister or an imam (great, for a need) which he is typically able to fulfill. So the mu'takif says) in the language of his state if not spoken by his tongue: (I will not leave) standing at the door of my Master, asking Him for all my needs and the removal of what has befallen me of distress. And my companion became to Him a cubit, and whoever comes to me walking, I come to him quickly. His saying: (for seeking refuge) is the cause for his saying 'honoring his guest' and 'as a favor', and what follows are circumstantial clauses. His saying: (and seeking fortification) with jarr conjoined to 'seeking refuge' and with nasb conjoined to 'emptying'. His saying: (so his enemy cannot reach him) - and he is Satan and the world. His saying: (and mighty support) i.e., His strength. He said in al-Qamus: 'a-yada-hu ta'yidan, he is mu'ayyad: I strengthened him.' His saying: 'you see subjects' (etc.) i.e., the Truth is more deserving of this station. His saying: (while he is an individual among them) i.e., he does not possess for himself harm or benefit, and it is a circumstantial clause. His saying: (the fulfilling of their needs) it can be plural or singular, and the first is more appropriate for the word 'subjects'. His saying: (with the might of his power) i.e., the sultan, and it is better to omit that because such expression befits only Allah. His saying: (and he indicated) i.e., the author. His saying: (on attaining the objective) it is better to omit 'attaining' i.e., on the objective of i'tikaf. His saying: (and removed the veil of doubt) i.e., the doubt which is like a veil, i.e., the doubt arising from some people concerning the result of i'tikaf. His saying: (and lifted the covering) conjoined to 'indicated'; the covering is the veil arising from doubt. His saying: (and manifested the truth) conjoined as a necessary consequence. His saying: (by an outpouring of giving) i.e., by a flood of giving, or by the giving that is like a flood. His saying: (the mujtahid) it conveys that he did not follow a specific imam from the four because they appeared after him. His saying: (most of the narrations of the Imam) i.e., his narrated traditions. His saying: 'thus in I'lam al-Akhyar' with kasra on alif of 'a'lam' apparently. His saying: (he said) he repeated it due to the distance of the first verb. His saying: (by his blessing) i.e., by his abundant good. His saying: (and his support) i.e., the support given to him of good things. His saying: (similitude) with fatha i.e., description. His saying: (or an Imam) includes the scholar contrary to what preceded. His saying: (the language) he said it i.e., his saying, and it is of the type of addition of the place to the state. His saying: (of distress) is what grips the soul of grief and sorrow. His saying: (and became) i.e., the distress
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah is an expansion on the ruling and virtues of i'tikaf (seclusion in the mosque for worship), particularly in the last ten days of Ramadan. The author, al-Tahtawi, begins by establishing that i'tikaf is a sunnah (recommended practice) supported by both transmitted and rational proofs.

The primary transmitted proof (dalil naqli) is the hadith of Abu Huraira and Aisha (may Allah be pleased with them) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) consistently observed i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan from his arrival in Medina until his death. This shows continuity and emphasis. Al-Zuhri's statement expresses amazement that people abandoned this practice despite the Prophet's persistence. The author notes that the Prophet once missed i'tikaf due to a specific incident involving his wives' tents in the mosque, and he made it up in Shawwal, indicating that i'tikaf is not wajib (obligatory) but rather a confirmed sunnah.

The rational proof (dalil aqli) is presented: i'tikaf is among the noblest acts because the mu'takif is waiting for prayer and receives the reward of one praying. It involves emptying the heart from worldly distractions, focusing solely on worship, and seeking closeness to Allah. The author elaborates on the virtues: it is a state of proximity to Allah, detachment from worldly pleasures, and fortification against the enemy (Satan and worldly desires).

The commentary then discusses the wording of the matn, explaining terms like 'al-mukhtassa' (specific) referring to mosques, and 'al-sunnah' meaning it is a communal sunnah (sunnah kifayah) that is highly emphasized (muakkadah). There is a minority view that it is merely mustahabb (recommended), but the relied-upon position (al-mu'tamad) is that it is sunnah muakkadah.

The author then quotes 'Ata' ibn Abi Rabah, a prominent tabi'i and teacher of Imam Abu Hanifa, who likens the mu'takif to a person standing persistently at the door of a king or great official seeking a need. This simile illustrates the mu'takif's state of humility, persistence, and complete reliance on Allah. The passage ends with more glosses on the text, explaining the figurative language and reinforcing the high status of i'tikaf.

Throughout, the commentary stays within the Hanafi school, clarifying the ruling, providing proofs, and emphasizing the spiritual benefits. The authority weight of the ruling (sunnah muakkadah) is based on the canonical hadith and is part of the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الاعتكاف (al-i'tikaf): Secluding oneself in a mosque for the purpose of worship, especially in the last ten days of Ramadan.
سنة كفاية (sunnah kifayah): A recommended act that if performed by a sufficient number of Muslims, the obligation of observing it is lifted from the rest of the community.
المعتمد (al-mu'tamad): The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, which is the basis for fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi ruling on i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan?
A) Fard 'ayn (individual obligation)
B) Sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah)
C) Mubah (permissible)
D) Haram (forbidden)

Key Takeaway
I'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan is an emphasized communal sunnah, established by the Prophet's continuous practice and rational virtues.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
The Mu'takif as a King's Petitioner
Commentary on the hadith describing the mu'takif as a petitioner at a king's door

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi view of the mu'takif's spiritual state as described by Ata ibn Abi Rabah and explain the significance of persistent supplication in i'tikaf.

Core Idea
The mu'takif is like a desperate petitioner at a king's door — i'tikaf is profound humility and persistent supplication.

This lesson explores a profound metaphor from the Tabi'i Imam Ata ibn Abi Rabah, a teacher of Imam Abu Hanifa. The mu'takif (one in spiritual seclusion) is compared to a man who stands repeatedly at the door of a great king to fulfill an important need. This metaphor illuminates the inner reality of i'tikaf as a state of utter dependence and earnest supplication before Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central metaphor
Read the hadith: 'The example of the mu'takif is like a man who goes back and forth and stands at the door of a king for a need.' Note the key elements: the man, the king's door, the persistence, and the need.
The king can represent Allah or a worldly authority, but the lesson focuses on the spiritual parallel.
2
Analyze the mu'takif's attitude
The mu'takif says by his state: 'I will not leave' until his needs are fulfilled. He remains standing at the door, asking for all his needs and removal of distresses. This shows complete reliance and desperation.
The commentary explains that the mu'takif is stripped of deeds and virtue, pleading with ilhah (persistence).
3
Understand the purpose of refuge and protection
The phrases 'lil-iltija' (for refuge) and 'al-tahassun' (fortified protection) indicate that the mu'takif seeks Allah's protection from the enemies of Shaytan and the world. This is the spiritual benefit of i'tikaf.
Al-tahassun is in the genitive as a conjoined noun to 'refuge,' showing it is part of the same act.
4
Relate the metaphor to the mu'takif's practice
In Hanafi fiqh, i'tikaf is sunnah mu'akkadah in the last ten days of Ramadan. The spiritual state described here is essential: the mu'takif should embody this humble supplication, not just physical seclusion.
The commentary emphasizes that the king ultimately represents Allah, as only He truly possesses power.

And said the master, the knower of Allah, the Imam, the mujtahid, Ata ibn Abi Rabah, the Tabi'i, student of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them), one of the teachers of the Great Imam (may Allah have mercy on him). Abu Hanifa said: I have not seen anyone more knowledgeable than Hammad, nor more comprehensive in knowledge than Ata ibn Abi Rabah. The majority of the narrations of the Great Imam Abu Hanifa are from Ata, who heard from Ibn Abbas, Ibn Umar, Abu Hurairah, Abu Sa'id, Jabir, and Aisha (may Allah be pleased with them). He died in the year 115 AH at the age of eighty, as found in I'lam al-Akhyar. He (Ata) said, may Allah have mercy on him and benefit us with his blessing and assistance: (The example of the one in i'tikaf is like a man who goes repeatedly) i.e., goes back and forth and stands at the door of a king, great minister, or imam (for a need) that can normally be fulfilled. So the one in i'tikaf says, by his state if not verbally: (I will not leave) standing at the door of my Master, asking Him for all my needs and the removal of the distresses that have befallen me. And my companion has become to me [like] a cubit? And whoever comes to me walking, I come to him running. The author's words: (for refuge) is the reason for saying 'honoring his guest' and 'as a favor,' and what follows are conditions. (and for fortified protection) in the genitive case as a conjoined noun to 'refuge,' and in the accusative as a conjoined noun to 'vacating.' (so his enemy does not reach him, and it is Shaytan and the world. (and mighty is His support) i.e., His strength. In al-Qamus: 'I supported him, so he is supported, I strengthened him.' (You see the subjects) i.e., the truth is more deserving of this position. (and he is one of them) i.e., he does not possess for himself harm or benefit, and it is a circumstantial clause. (the fulfillment of their needs) can be plural or singular, and the first is more appropriate for the word 'subjects.' (towards achieving the desired goal) it is better to omit 'achieving' i.e., towards the desired goal of i'tikaf. (and he removed the veil of doubt) i.e., the doubt that was like a veil, i.e., the doubt arising from some people regarding the fruit of i'tikaf. (and he removed the covering) conjoined to 'pointed out,' and by 'covering' is meant the veil arising from doubt. (and he manifested the truth) is a necessary conjoined clause. (through the abundance of giving) i.e., through the abundance of the Giver, or through the giving that is like an abundance. (therefore my dearest brothers, nay, my closest relatives, so that He forgives me my sins which are the cause of my distance and the descent of my calamities. Then He bestows upon me, out of His favor, that which befits His worthiness and generosity, as an honor to one who sought refuge in His impregnable fortress and the protection of His sanctuary. And this is an indication that the servant who has compiled these issues stands in the position of a lowly servant at the door of his Master, stripped of deeds and the ascription of virtues, turning to Him, the Exalted, with the greatest of means, with the palms of neediness, insisting in supplication and questions, thrown upon the threshold of Allah's door, hoping for His intercession tomorrow before Him by what He has promised, and He is the Guarantor of all good.
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a beautiful statement attributed to the great Tabi'i Imam 'Ata ibn Abi Rabah, describing the state of a person performing i'tikaf (spiritual retreat in the mosque). The passage begins with a brief biographical note establishing 'Ata's credentials as a teacher of Abu Hanifa and a prolific narrator. The core of the lesson is a metaphorical comparison: the mu'takif is like a man who stands persistently at the door of a great king, minister, or scholar to fulfill an important need. The mu'takif's condition is that he does not leave, humbly asking for all his needs and the removal of his distresses. The commentary then explains various phrases: 'lil-'ltija'' (for refuge) is the cause for honor and favor; 'al-tahassun' (fortified protection) protects from the enemies of Shaytan and the world. The commentator emphasizes that the king in the metaphor ultimately represents Allah, as only He truly possesses power and honor. The passage then shifts to the author's personal reflection, where he identifies himself as the 'servant who has compiled these issues,' standing at Allah's door with humility, stripped of any claim to deeds or virtue, pleading with insistence (il-hah) for forgiveness and divine favor. The lesson concludes with hope in Allah's promise and guarantee of all good. This passage teaches that i'tikaf is not merely physical seclusion but a state of profound spiritual humility and desperate need for Allah's mercy.

Key Terms
المعتكف (al-mu'takif): The person performing i'tikaf, spiritual seclusion in a mosque for worship, typically during Ramadan.
الالتجاء (al-iltija'): Seeking refuge or shelter, especially in a fortified place.
الإلحاح (al-ilhah): Persistence and earnestness in supplication (du'a).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith of Ata ibn Abi Rabah, what does the mu'takif resemble?
A) A king on his throne
B) A servant standing at the door of a king for his need
C) A warrior in battle
D) A merchant trading in the marketplace

Key Takeaway
The mu'takif's state is that of a humble petitioner at the King's door, never leaving without his need fulfilled.
